Understanding Circular Motion as Non-Directional
Gloss explaining circular motion's non-directionality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why circular motion does not count as a motion towards a direction in the argument about heaviness and lightness.

Core Idea
Circular motion involves no change of place, hence no directionality.

The second gloss addresses a key concept in the heavy-light argument: circular motion. The commentator explains that conventional circular motion is defined by the moving body not leaving its place. This means it does not involve motion in the sense of directional change, which is crucial for refuting objections that might try to attribute direction to celestial motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the definition and implication.
Notice the condition 'that the moving body does not leave its place'.
2
Understand the condition of circular motion
Focus on the phrase 'مشروطة بأن لا يخرج المتحرك عن حيزه' and why this matters.
This condition makes circular motion different from rectilinear motion.
3
See why it precludes direction
The gloss concludes that circular motion does not necessitate direction. Think about why staying in one place means no preferred direction.
4
Connect to the argument
Relate this to the larger argument: if celestial spheres undergo circular motion, they have no natural direction, so they cannot be heavy or light.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لأنه شفاف إلخ) في الشفاء غاية الجسم السماوي مشف ينفذ فيه البصر، وهذا الحكم بديهي يحكم به العقل بمعونة الحس ولا يرد عليه شيء من الاعتراضات المذكورة، فإنه ليس المراد بالمشف ما لا لون له أصلاً، بل ما ينفذ فيه البصر ولو كان ملوناً.

قوله : (فإنها تتحرك على الاستقامة إلخ) لا يخفى أن الحركة المستديرة الاصطلاحية مشروطة بأن لا يخرج المتحرك عن حيزه، فلا حركة على الاستدارة فيها وبهذا ظهر أن الحركة المستديرة تقتضي عدم التوجه إلى الجهة لأنها غير مقتضية للتوجه إليها.

قوله : ( بين الميلين ) أي بين الميلين أي بين المدافعتين.

قوله : ( فإن الحجر إلخ) فيه أن المراد أنه لا يكون فيه مبدأ ميل مستقيم طبيعياً لامتناع أن يقتضي الطبيعية الواحدة لو خليت وطبعه الأمرين المتنافيين وإذا لم يكن طبيعياً لم يكن قسرياً لما تقرر أنه حيث لا طبع لا قسر ولا يكون في الأفلاك مبدأ ميل مستقيم لا طبيعياً ولا قسرياً.
His saying: '(because it is transparent etc.)' In the Shifa, the celestial body is transparent, the vision penetrates it, and this judgment is self-evident judged by reason with the help of sense, and none of the mentioned objections apply to it, for what is meant by 'transparent' is not that which has no color at all, but that which vision penetrates even if it is colored.

His saying: '(for it moves in a straight line etc.)' It is not hidden that conventional circular motion is conditioned by the moving body not leaving its place, so there is no motion upon circularity in it, and by this it appears that circular motion necessitates non-direction to a direction because it does not necessitate direction towards it.

His saying: '(between the two inclinations)' i.e., between the two inclinations, i.e., between the two thrusts.

His saying: '(for the stone etc.)' In it is that what is meant is that there is not in it a principle of rectilinear inclination naturally, because it is impossible that a single nature, if left to itself and its nature, necessitates two incompatible things. And if it is not natural, it is not forced, because it is established that where there is no nature, there is no force. And in the celestial spheres, there is no principle of rectilinear inclination either naturally or forcibly.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage contains four marginal glosses (قوله) commenting on the previous argument about heaviness and lightness. The first gloss clarifies the meaning of 'transparent' (شفاف) in the context of the celestial body: it does not mean colorless but that vision can penetrate it, even if colored. The second gloss explains that conventional circular motion is defined by the body not leaving its place, so it does not involve motion on a circle in the sense of changing location; thus circular motion does not direct the body to any direction. The third gloss is a brief note equating 'inclinations' (ميلين) with 'thrusts' (مدافعتين). The fourth gloss explains the stone example: a natural rectilinear inclination cannot coexist with a circular inclination because a single nature cannot produce two incompatible inclinations; since the celestial spheres have no natural rectilinear inclination, they also have no forced one.

Key Terms
حركة مستديرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does circular motion not involve directionality according to the gloss?
A) Because it is too fast
B) Because the body does not leave its place
C) Because it is combined with straight motion
D) Because it is imaginary

Key Takeaway
Circular motion is non-directional because it does not involve leaving one's place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that not all motion leads to a change of place. In your spiritual life, is your daily 'motion' (prayers, learning, actions) actually moving you closer to Allah, or are you just rotating in the same place? What would it take for you to truly leave your spiritual stagnation and move directionally towards Him?
?
Circular motion keeps you in your ḥayyiz (place). What is the 'place' of your heart? Consider your attachments, habits, and priorities. Are you content to stay there, or is there a direction you must follow to break free and progress? What step will you take today to leave that place and head towards a deeper relationship with your Creator?

Equating Inclinations with Thrusts
Brief gloss equating inclinations with thrusts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in this context, 'inclinations' (ميلين) are synonymous with 'thrusts' (مدافعتين).

Core Idea
Inclinations here mean thrusts, opposing forces.

This very short gloss simply equates two terms: 'the two inclinations' (الميلين) and 'the two thrusts' (المدافعتين). This is a technical clarification to ensure the reader understands that the argument is about opposing forces, not some other kind of inclination.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss
Read the Arabic and translation to see the direct equation.
Notice the shorthand: 'i.e., between the two thrusts'.
2
Note the equivalence
The gloss explicitly says 'أي بين المدافعتين'. This is a terminological identification.
3
Understand why this clarification is needed
Reflect on how 'inclination' might be ambiguous and why the author wants to specify it as a thrust in this argument.
In physics, 'inclination' could mean tendency, but here it's about opposing forces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

No Rectilinear Inclination in Celestial Spheres
Gloss arguing against rectilinear inclination in celestial spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why celestial spheres cannot have a natural rectilinear inclination, and thus no forced inclination either.

Core Idea
A single nature cannot produce two incompatible inclinations.

The final gloss addresses the stone example, arguing that celestial bodies cannot have a principle of rectilinear inclination. The reasoning is that a single nature, if left to itself, cannot necessitate two incompatible inclinations. Therefore, since there is no natural rectilinear inclination, there cannot be a forced one either. This supports the claim that celestial motion is neither straight nor forced.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss
Read the Arabic passage and translation to follow the logical argument.
Pay attention to logical connectors: 'lam...fa...'.
2
Understand the impossibility of natural rectilinear inclination
The gloss argues that a single nature cannot necessitate two incompatible things. Think about what 'incompatible' means here (e.g., straight and circular motion).
3
See the consequence for forced inclination
If there is no natural inclination, there can be no forced inclination, because force presupposes a nature to act upon. The gloss states 'حيث لا طبع لا قسر'.
4
Apply to celestial spheres
The conclusion: celestial spheres have no rectilinear inclination at all. Consider how this supports the argument that their motion is purely circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لأنه شفاف إلخ) في الشفاء غاية الجسم السماوي مشف ينفذ فيه البصر، وهذا الحكم بديهي يحكم به العقل بمعونة الحس ولا يرد عليه شيء من الاعتراضات المذكورة، فإنه ليس المراد بالمشف ما لا لون له أصلاً، بل ما ينفذ فيه البصر ولو كان ملوناً.

قوله : (فإنها تتحرك على الاستقامة إلخ) لا يخفى أن الحركة المستديرة الاصطلاحية مشروطة بأن لا يخرج المتحرك عن حيزه، فلا حركة على الاستدارة فيها وبهذا ظهر أن الحركة المستديرة تقتضي عدم التوجه إلى الجهة لأنها غير مقتضية للتوجه إليها.

قوله : ( بين الميلين ) أي بين الميلين أي بين المدافعتين.

قوله : ( فإن الحجر إلخ) فيه أن المراد أنه لا يكون فيه مبدأ ميل مستقيم طبيعياً لامتناع أن يقتضي الطبيعية الواحدة لو خليت وطبعه الأمرين المتنافيين وإذا لم يكن طبيعياً لم يكن قسرياً لما تقرر أنه حيث لا طبع لا قسر ولا يكون في الأفلاك مبدأ ميل مستقيم لا طبيعياً ولا قسرياً.
His saying: '(because it is transparent etc.)' In the Shifa, the celestial body is transparent, the vision penetrates it, and this judgment is self-evident judged by reason with the help of sense, and none of the mentioned objections apply to it, for what is meant by 'transparent' is not that which has no color at all, but that which vision penetrates even if it is colored.

His saying: '(for it moves in a straight line etc.)' It is not hidden that conventional circular motion is conditioned by the moving body not leaving its place, so there is no motion upon circularity in it, and by this it appears that circular motion necessitates non-direction to a direction because it does not necessitate direction towards it.

His saying: '(between the two inclinations)' i.e., between the two inclinations, i.e., between the two thrusts.

His saying: '(for the stone etc.)' In it is that what is meant is that there is not in it a principle of rectilinear inclination naturally, because it is impossible that a single nature, if left to itself and its nature, necessitates two incompatible things. And if it is not natural, it is not forced, because it is established that where there is no nature, there is no force. And in the celestial spheres, there is no principle of rectilinear inclination either naturally or forcibly.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage contains four marginal glosses (قوله) commenting on the previous argument about heaviness and lightness. The first gloss clarifies the meaning of 'transparent' (شفاف) in the context of the celestial body: it does not mean colorless but that vision can penetrate it, even if colored. The second gloss explains that conventional circular motion is defined by the body not leaving its place, so it does not involve motion on a circle in the sense of changing location; thus circular motion does not direct the body to any direction. The third gloss is a brief note equating 'inclinations' (ميلين) with 'thrusts' (مدافعتين). The fourth gloss explains the stone example: a natural rectilinear inclination cannot coexist with a circular inclination because a single nature cannot produce two incompatible inclinations; since the celestial spheres have no natural rectilinear inclination, they also have no forced one.

Key Terms
ميل
طبيعي
قسري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial bodies have a natural rectilinear inclination like heavy objects.
Correction: They cannot have such an inclination because their nature would be incompatible with circular motion, and since no nature for rectilinear motion, no forced motion either.

Checkpoint
Why can't celestial spheres have a natural rectilinear inclination?
A) Because they are too far away
B) Because a single nature cannot necessitate two incompatible things
C) Because they are made of fire
D) Because they are perfectly round

Key Takeaway
Celestial spheres lack any rectilinear inclination because a single nature cannot produce incompatible inclinations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that 'a single nature cannot necessitate two incompatible things'. Consider your own heart: are there incompatible desires or loyalties pulling you in different directions? What would it take to bring your inner nature into alignment with a single, godly purpose?
?
'Where there is no nature, there is no force.' In your spiritual practice, do you find yourself forcing actions (like prayer or charity) without first cultivating a natural inclination from within? How can you nurture a genuine love for these acts so that they become effortless?
?
If the celestial spheres are free from contradictory inclinations, what does that say about the consistency of your own character? Are you striving to be a person of integrity, where your inner nature consistently produces actions that please Allah?

Scope of Denial of Heat and Cold
Commentator clarifies the scope of heat/cold denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the claim that celestial spheres lack heat and cold is limited to the elemental kind, not all heat and cold.

Core Idea
Celestial spheres lack elemental heat/cold, not all heat/cold.

This gloss addresses a potential objection by clarifying the precise meaning of the denial of heat and cold in the celestial spheres. The commentator explains that only the kind found in elements is negated, as the goal is to show qualitative difference, not absence of any heat or cold.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Note the key phrase 'this heat and cold that exist in the elements'.
2
Identify the qualifier
Identify the phrase that limits the denial: 'the denial of this heat and cold that exist in the elements'. Notice the contrast with heat and cold of a different nature.
The commentator distinguishes two kinds of heat/cold.
3
Connect to the broader argument
Ask yourself how this clarification supports the purpose of showing the difference between celestial spheres and elements in qualities and effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولمانع أن يمنع إلخ) هذا مدفوع لأن المدعى نفى هذه الحرارة والبرودة الموجودتين في العناصر، وأما الحرارة والبرودة المخالفتين بالحقيقة والآثار لهاتين، فلا يتعلق غرضنا بنفسهما وإثباتهما ، إذ المقصود بيان مخالفة الأفلاك للعناصر بالكيفيات والآثار .

قوله : ( أي التالي إلخ يعني أن قوله : ولا استحالة عطف على قوله : وإلا لكانت بحـ المعنى .
His saying: '(And an objector may deny etc.)' This is refuted because what is claimed is the denial of this heat and cold that exist in the elements. As for heat and cold that are different in reality and effects from these two, we do not aim to negate them or prove them, for the purpose is to show the difference of the celestial spheres from the elements in qualities and effects.

His saying: '(i.e., the consequent etc.)' Means that his saying 'and it is impossible' is conjoined to his saying 'otherwise it would be...' in meaning.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage contains two marginal glosses on the heat-cold argument. The first gloss explains that the denial of heat and cold in the celestial spheres is specifically about the kind of heat and cold found in the elements (i.e., that are correlated with heaviness and lightness). Heat and cold of a different nature and effects are not being negated; the goal is to show the qualitative difference between the celestial and elemental realms. The second gloss is a textual note clarifying the grammatical connection of the phrase 'and it is impossible' to the previous 'otherwise it would be...'.

Key Terms
حرارة
برودة
عناصر
كيفيات
آثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the celestial spheres have absolutely no heat or cold at all.
Correction: The gloss specifies that only the elemental kind of heat and cold is denied; other kinds are not negated.

Checkpoint
According to the first gloss, what kind of heat and cold are denied in the celestial spheres?
A) All heat and cold whatsoever.
B) Only the kind that exists in the elements.
C) Only heat and cold that are caused by motion.
D) Only the heat and cold that reach the Earth.

Key Takeaway
The celestial spheres are only denied elemental heat and cold, not all heat and cold.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a theological claim you hold with certainty. Have you ever examined whether your understanding is too simplistic—like assuming that 'heat' means the same everywhere? This lesson forces you to ask: which of your beliefs need more careful qualification before you defend them? Will you take time today to reconsider one such belief with precision?
?
When someone challenges your view, do you immediately reject the objection or do you first clarify the scope of your claim? The commentator refines the scope rather than dismissing the objector outright. Where in your life are you missing the opportunity to gain deeper understanding because you refuse to nuance your position? Commit to one area where you will practice intellectual humility this week.

Grammatical Note on Conjoined Phrase
Commentator notes grammatical conjunction of two phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the commentator explains the grammatical connection between the phrase 'it is impossible' and a preceding conditional statement.

Core Idea
A grammatical conjunction clarifies the logical flow of the argument.

This short gloss addresses a textual connection. The commentator notes that the phrase 'and it is impossible' is grammatically conjoined to the preceding 'otherwise it would be...'. Recognizing such conjunctions is crucial for accurately parsing the original argument's structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the commentator identifies a grammatical relationship.
2
Identify the conjoined elements
Identify the two parts: 'and it is impossible' (ولا استحالة) and the previous phrase 'otherwise it would be...' (وإلا لكانت).
The commentator uses 'عطف' meaning grammatical conjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولمانع أن يمنع إلخ) هذا مدفوع لأن المدعى نفى هذه الحرارة والبرودة الموجودتين في العناصر، وأما الحرارة والبرودة المخالفتين بالحقيقة والآثار لهاتين، فلا يتعلق غرضنا بنفسهما وإثباتهما ، إذ المقصود بيان مخالفة الأفلاك للعناصر بالكيفيات والآثار .

قوله : ( أي التالي إلخ يعني أن قوله : ولا استحالة عطف على قوله : وإلا لكانت بحـ المعنى .
His saying: '(And an objector may deny etc.)' This is refuted because what is claimed is the denial of this heat and cold that exist in the elements. As for heat and cold that are different in reality and effects from these two, we do not aim to negate them or prove them, for the purpose is to show the difference of the celestial spheres from the elements in qualities and effects.

His saying: '(i.e., the consequent etc.)' Means that his saying 'and it is impossible' is conjoined to his saying 'otherwise it would be...' in meaning.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage contains two marginal glosses on the heat-cold argument. The first gloss explains that the denial of heat and cold in the celestial spheres is specifically about the kind of heat and cold found in the elements (i.e., that are correlated with heaviness and lightness). Heat and cold of a different nature and effects are not being negated; the goal is to show the qualitative difference between the celestial and elemental realms. The second gloss is a textual note clarifying the grammatical connection of the phrase 'and it is impossible' to the previous 'otherwise it would be...'.

Key Terms
عطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the phrase 'ولا استحالة'?
A) It is the beginning of a new sentence.
B) It is conjoined to 'وإلا لكانت'.
C) It is a parenthetical remark.
D) It is a separate argument.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'it is impossible' is grammatically linked to the previous conditional statement, not standalone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a complex theological argument, do you take the time to notice grammatical connectors like 'and' and 'but'? How might a missed conjunction change your understanding of the author's point? Commit to reading one passage this week with full attention to every connecting word.
?
This lesson shows a small grammatical detail that prevents misunderstanding. Think of a time you misunderstood an important text because you overlooked a small word. What did that cost you? Will you now slow down and examine every phrase, even when you feel rushed?
?
The commentator's note seems trivial, but it guards against misinterpreting the entire argument. Where in your own life have you dismissed a small detail only to later regret it? What will you do today to honor the precision required for truly understanding Allah's words and the scholars' explanations?

Marginal Notes on Appropriateness, Position, and Circular Motion
Commentary on the terms 'أولى من عكسه', 'وضعه', 'وربما قالوا', and the objection about circular motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced clarifications of key terms in the circular motion argument, including 'awla min 'aksihi', 'wad'', and the classification of bodily states.

Core Idea
Marginal notes sharpen theological arguments by defining terms precisely.

The commentary on the circular motion argument includes several marginal notes that clarify the meaning of key phrases. These notes explain when appropriateness is conceded or denied, define 'position' as alignment of parts, classify bodily states as necessary or contingent, and address an objection involving circular motion. This lesson walks through each clarification to deepen your grasp of the argument's logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without analyzing. Get a sense of the four distinct notes.
Notice that each note begins with 'قوله' (his saying).
2
Examine the first note on 'أولى من عكسه'
Understand the conditional: if 'absence of appropriateness' refers to specific essence, it is conceded; if absolute, it is rejected because particularities can confer appropriateness.
This nuance prevents a blanket denial of priority.
3
Study the definition of 'الوضع' (position)
Position is defined as the state arising from the relation of parts to what contains them, specifically their alignment.
This definition comes from Sharh al-Isharat.
4
Work through the third note on bodily states
Two types: necessary by nature (immutable) and contingent (caused by efficient causes, subject to change). Understand that ceasing is by nature, possibility by causes.
This classification underpins the possibility of motion.
5
Analyze the fourth note on circular motion
If circular motion were not impossible, it would imply motion without the 'excess' (al-fā'iq), which is absurd. But the proof against it is weak.
This note hints at a dialectical exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أولى من عكسه إن أراد عدم الأولوية نظراً إلى الماهية النوعية للأجزاء فمسلم عدم. وإن أراد عدم الأولوية مطلقاً فممنوع لجواز أن يكون الأولوية ناشئة من خصوصية كل واحد من أجزاء الجرم بالقياس إلى كل واحد من أجزاء الحيز . قوله : ( في وضعه إلخ وهو الهيئة التي تعرض بحسب نسبة أجزائه إلى ما هو داخل فيه وهو محاذاتها له كذا في شرح الإشارات . قوله : ( وربما قالوا إلخ) تفصيله ما في الإشارات من أن أحوال الجسم لا تخلو، إما أن يجب بحسب طبعه أو لا يجب، بل يمكن والواجب طبعه لا يمكن أن يتبدل أو يزول وغير الواجبية إنما يحصل للجسم بحسب علل فاعلية تقتضيه وتلك الأحوال قابلة للتبدل، فالزائل بالنظر إلى طباع الجسم، وليس لقابله بهما بالنظر إلى عللها ما دامت مانعة عن التبدل والزوال، وإذا كانت الحال في الموضع والوضع هذه أمكن انتقال الجسم عنها باعتبار طبعه فأمكن أن يزيل قوله : ( وإلا امتنعت حركة المستديرة إذ لو لم يمتنع حركته المستديرة يلزم من فرض وقوعه أن تكون الحركة مع الفائق كهي لا معه، وقد مر دليل بطلانه وقد مر أيضاً ضعف هذا الدليل.
His saying: 'More appropriate than its opposite' — if he means the absence of appropriateness with respect to the specific essence of the parts, then the absence is conceded. But if he means the absence of appropriateness absolutely, it is rejected because of the possibility that the appropriateness arises from the particularity of each one of the parts of the body in relation to each one of the parts of the place. His saying: 'Regarding its position etc.' — and it is the state that occurs according to the relation of its parts to what is within it, which is their alignment to it, as in Sharh al-Isharat. His saying: 'And perhaps they said etc.' — its detail is in al-Isharat: that the states of the body are either necessary by its nature or not necessary but possible. What is necessary by nature cannot change or cease. What is non-necessary occurs for the body through efficient causes that necessitate it, and those states are subject to change. So the ceasing [of a state] is with respect to the nature of the body, and its possibility is with respect to its causes as long as they prevent change and cessation. And when the state of position and place is like this, it becomes possible for the body to depart from it in consideration of its nature, so it is possible for it to cease. His saying: 'Otherwise circular motion would be impossible' — for if its circular motion were not impossible, then from the hypothesis of its occurrence it would follow that the motion is without the excess as it is not without it, and the proof of its falsity has passed, and the weakness of this proof has also passed.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This lesson collects four marginal notes. The first clarifies the phrase 'more appropriate than its opposite' (awla min 'aksihi): if appropriateness is denied with respect to specific essence, it is conceded; but absolute denial is rejected because particulars could confer appropriateness. The second defines 'position' (wadʿ) as the state arising from the alignment of parts to what contains them. The third elaborates on the possible states of a body: those necessary by nature are immutable; those contingent arise from efficient causes and can change. The fourth discusses the objection that if circular motion were not impossible, it would entail an absurdity (motion without 'excess'), but the proof against it is weak.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'أولى من عكسه' always denies any appropriateness.
Correction: It only denies appropriateness when considering specific essence; particularities can establish appropriateness.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The four marginal notes collectively refine the key terms and logical moves in the circular motion argument, showing the importance of precise definitions in kalam.

Proof of Circular Motion via Contingent Position
Author argues celestial parts must have circular motion due to contingent position, with objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency of celestial positions leads to the necessity of circular motion, and how an objection based on simplicity is addressed.

Core Idea
Contingent positions in the heavens necessitate circular motion.

This passage presents a key argument for celestial motion: since each part of the sphere occupies a specific place but is essentially equal to other parts, its position must be contingent rather than necessary, implying the ability to move to another's place — which is circular motion. An objection based on the assumption of simplicity is raised and answered within the commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic original and its translation. Notice the structure: the author poses a dichotomy (necessary or contingent), rules out necessity, deduces contingency, then infers circular motion.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الأمور المتساوية في الماهية' — this is the core premise.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that the proof assumes simplicity of the spheres, which has not been proven for spheres other than the outermost. Identify where this appears in the passage.
The objection is introduced with 'والإشكال عليه'.
3
Understand the response in the commentary
The commentary argues that the proof does not require simplicity but rather the possibility of motion and the presence of inclination. Read the commentary to see how the objection is deflected.
The response emphasizes that the proof's essence is capability of circular motion, not simplicity.
4
Reflect on the broader implications
Consider how this argument contributes to proving the existence of a mover and the nature of celestial bodies. This connects to the larger theme of divine transcendence.

وذلك لأن ما لا ميل طبيعياً فيه لا يقبل حركة من خارج أصلاً . قال في الشفاء بعد بيان ما لا ميل له، لا يقبل الحركة من خارج أن كل جسم يطرأ عليه ما لم تكن مبدأها فيه بالطبع بل يصدر عنه بسبب خارج أو نفس متحرك بالاستدارة واللازم منه أن يكون متحركاً بالاستدارة مطلقاً، وأما خصوصية جهة الحركة والقطبين والسرعة والبطء فهو سبب الحركة بخصوصيته له مع ذلك، وإن لم تكن
The specification of each part of the celestial sphere to a particular position and place must be either necessary or contingent. The first is impossible, because for things equal in essence it is impossible that some should have what others do not. So the second is determined, which implies the validity of each part moving to the position and place of another, and that is by circular motion. So circular motion is possible for the sphere, and it contains a principle of circular inclination. Otherwise circular motion would be impossible. And everything that contains a principle of circular inclination moves circularly, because the effect must exist when its cause exists. (And the objection to it) i.e., to the first way mentioned in the book, for it is based on simplicity, and you have not proven simplicity for the spheres other than the outermost, so your proof here falls short of your claim. (And if granted)... [commentary follows]
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument that celestial spheres must have circular motion because their positions are contingent, not necessary. The author argues that if all parts are equal in essence, then necessary position is impossible; thus each part can move to another's position, which requires circular motion. This implies a principle of circular inclination in the sphere. An objection is raised: this proof assumes the spheres are simple, but simplicity has not been proven for spheres other than the outermost. The commentary then explains the objection and defends the proof, stating that the proof does not require simplicity but rather relies on the possibility of motion and the presence of inclination. The response includes that the proof's essence is that the sphere is capable of circular motion, and any capable thing has the inclination; the specifics of direction, poles, speed are external causes.

Misconception: The proof of circular motion depends on the spheres being simple bodies.
Correction: The proof actually depends on the contingency of positions, not on simplicity. Simplicity is not required for the argument to hold.

Why a Simple Body Cannot Move in a Circle
Proof that a simple body cannot move in a circle and necessitates a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that a simple body cannot move in a circle without implying a free agent, and why this leads to affirming divine free will.

Core Idea
A simple body's circular motion would require unjustified preferences, proving the mover must be a free agent.

This passage presents a classic kalam proof against necessitarian theories of causation. It argues that a simple (uniform) body cannot undergo circular motion unless a free agent specifies which parts move where. Understanding this argument helps clarify why Muslim theologians affirmed that Allah is a free agent, not a mere necessitating cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Try to follow the logical flow of the argument without worrying about every detail.
The argument is dense; reading it twice helps.
2
Understand the dilemma of circular motion
The author presents a dilemma: if a simple body moves circularly, it must move either to all sides at once (impossible) or to some sides without others (unjustified preference). Grasp why both options are problematic.
This is a key step.
3
Examine the pole and circle argument
Next, notice the argument about poles and varying circles. Because all points are identical, any specific assignment requires a preponderator. Identify why a necessitating agent cannot provide this.
4
Conclude that the mover is a free agent
Finally, see how the argument forces the conclusion that the mover must be a free agent (mukhtar) who specifies by will, and how this relieves many philosophical burdens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم ثبوت البساطة في الكل قلنا : هي لا تقتضي الحركة بالاستدارة، بل تقتضي عدمها لأن البسيط إذا تحرك كذلك ) فإما أن يتحرك إلى جميع الجهات ) أي الجوانب دفعة واحدة ( وأنه محال أو إلى بعضها دون بعض ( وإنه ترجيح بلا مرجح) كما أن سكونه كذلك عندكم ، ( وأيضاً ) إذا تحرك البسيط على الاستدارة ( فلا بد ) هناك ( من قطبين) معينين ( ساكنين و ) من (دوائر) مخصوصة متفاوتة جداً في الصغر والكبر ) ترسمها الأجزاء ) والنقط المفروضة فيما بينهما ( حولهما بحركات مختلفة ) اختلافاً عظيماً بالسرعة والبطء مع استواء جميع النقط ( المفروضة ( فيه ) أي في البسيط ) وصلاحيتها للقطبية والسكون ورسم الدائرة الصغيرة أو الكبيرة بالحركة البطيئة أو السريعة ( وأنه ترجيح بلا (مرجح كما لا يخفى على ذي بصيرة (فلا يمكن إسناد ذلك ) أي تعيين بعض النقط للقطبية وبعضها لرسم الدائرة (إلى) فاعل ) موجب بالذات لأنه لا تخصيص من الموجب (إلا) لمرجح معد للقابل) فينتقل الكلام إليه (و) أيضاً نسبته إلى جميع الأجزاء سواء ) فلا يتصور منه تخصيص وتعيين فيما بينها ( بل إلى مختار ) يفعل ما يشاء بمجرد إرادته من غير احتياج إلى كما داع مرجح مر . ( وإذا وجب الرجوع بالآخرة إلى فعل المختار فليعترفوا به أولاً، فإنه يخفف عنهم كثيرا من المؤونات ) التي تلزمهم لإثبات قواعدهم الحكمية خصوصاً في أحكام الأفلاك، فإن تلك المؤونات مبنية على كون الواجب موجباً بالذات، فإذا قيل : إنه مختار سقطت
The First Observatory - Chapter Two: Section One: Second Intention: On the directions of the body. The affirmation of simplicity in the whole. We said: It does not necessitate motion in a circle, but rather necessitates its absence, because if the simple body moves in such a way, then either it moves towards all directions (i.e., sides) at once, and that is impossible, or towards some of them without others, and that is preponderance without a preponderator, just as its rest is likewise according to you. And also, if the simple body moves in a circle, there must necessarily be two specific poles that are stationary and specific circles, extremely varying in smallness and largeness, which the parts and points supposed between them trace around them with different motions, greatly differing in speed and slowness, while all the supposed points (in it, i.e., in the simple body) are equal and their suitability for being poles and rest and drawing the small or large circle by slow or fast motion, and that is preponderance without a preponderator, as is not hidden from one of insight. So it is not possible to attribute that (i.e., the assigning of some points to be poles and others to draw the circle) to an agent that is necessitating by essence, because the necessitating agent does not specify except for a preponderator that prepares the receiver, so the speech transfers to it. And also its relation to all parts is equal, so specification and determination among them cannot be conceived from it. Rather, to a free agent who does whatever He wills by His mere will, without need for a perfect cause? (unclear). And when the final recourse is to the action of the free agent, they should admit it first, for it relieves them of many burdens that are necessary for them to establish their philosophical principles, especially in the rulings of the celestial spheres, for those burdens are based on the necessary being being necessitating by essence. So if it is said that He is free, they fall away.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a proof that a simple body (بسيط) cannot move in a circular path. The argument proceeds: A simple body is uniform, so if it moves circularly, it must either move to all directions at once (impossible) or to some without others, which is unjustified preference (ترجيح بلا مرجح). Circular motion also requires specific poles and varying circles, requiring further unjustified specifications. Since all points in the simple body are identical, specification cannot come from a necessitating agent (موجب بالذات) that acts uniformly; it must come from a free agent (مختار) who specifies by will. The conclusion is that admitting a free agent relieves many philosophical difficulties about the celestial spheres.

بسيط
ترجيح بلا مرجح
موجب بالذات
مختار
البساطة
الاستدارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a simple body can move in a circle if it is perfectly homogeneous.
Correction: The argument shows that homogeneity actually makes such motion impossible without a specifying cause, because all parts are equal so any specific motion requires an external free agent.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if a simple body were to move in a circle, what would be the consequence for the nature of the mover?
A) The mover must be a necessitating agent.
B) The mover must be a free agent.
C) The mover must be a body.
D) The mover must be immaterial.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of a simple body moving in a circle without unjustified preferences proves that the mover must be a free agent, which affirms God's free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of God as being 'forced' by His own nature to act in a certain way, as if He had no choice? How does this argument challenge that view and what would change in your trust if you truly saw Him as free?
?
What 'preferences' in your own life do you attribute to mere luck or circumstance, when really they should drive you to acknowledge a wise, free Agent? How can you let that realization shape your gratitude and reliance on God today?
?
If you admit that God is a free agent who acts without necessity, what area of your life are you still trying to control as if He must comply with your plans? What would it cost you to surrender that to His free will?

Objection and Answer on Circular Motion and Inclination
Objection and answer on circular motion and inclination principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that circular motion requires an actual principle of circular inclination is refuted through the distinction between possibility and actuality and the counterexample of a rolling sphere.

Core Idea
Circular motion only needs possibility of inclination, not actuality; inclinations can coexist.

This passage presents a specific objection against the second aspect of a previous argument regarding simple bodies. The objector claims that circular motion forces the existence of an actual principle of circular inclination. The author refutes this by clarifying that only the possibility of such a principle is required, and that the effect may be delayed by obstacles. He also rejects the claim that straight and circular inclinations are incompatible, offering concrete examples where they combine.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Notice the objection and the author's response.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the phrase 'لا وجوده بالفعل'.
2
Identify the core objection
The objection states that valid circular motion implies the actual existence of a principle of circular inclination. Find where this is asserted.
3
Analyze the two parts of the answer
The answer first distinguishes between possibility and actuality, then argues that obstacles can prevent the effect. The second part addresses incompatibility.
Look for the examples: rolling sphere (الكرة المدحرجة) and wheel (العجلة).
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves the simplicity of celestial bodies while explaining their motion without positing unnecessary actual principles.
This connects to broader discussions in kalam about causality and the nature of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإشكال على الوجه الثاني : فهو أنه أيضاً مبني على البساطة فيرد عليه ما ورد على الأول مع شيء زائد هو أن صحة الحركة المستديرة تستلزم صحة وجود مبدأ الميل المستدير لا وجوده بالفعل، وأن وجود المؤثر قد يتخلف عنه الأثر لوجود المانع ( ومنها أنه ليس فيه مبدأ ميل مستقيم لمنافاته للميل المستدير) كما مر . ( وقد عرفت ما فيه وهو أنه لا منافاة بينهما لاجتماعهما في الكرة المدحرجة والعجلة
As for the problem [regarding] the second aspect, it is that it is also based on simplicity, so what was said about the first applies to it, with something additional: that the validity of circular motion entails the validity of the existence of the principle of circular inclination, not its actual existence, and that the existence of the effect may be delayed from the cause due to the existence of an obstacle. And among them is that it has no principle of straight inclination, because it is incompatible with circular inclination, as has passed. And you have known what is in it: that there is no incompatibility between them because they combine in a rolling sphere and a wheel.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (إشكال) on a second aspect and its answer. The objection claims that circular motion of a simple body requires the actual presence of a principle of circular inclination (مبدأ الميل المستدير). The answer is twofold: first, the validity of circular motion only requires the possibility of such an inclination, not its actual existence, and the effect may be delayed due to obstacles. Second, the claim that a simple body cannot have both straight and circular inclinations is refuted by the counterexample of a rolling sphere (كرة مدحرجة) and a wheel (عجلة), where both inclinations coexist. Therefore, the objection fails.

Key Terms

مبدأ الميل المستقيم
الكرة المدحرجة
العجلة
منافاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think circular motion requires an actual existing inclination.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only the possibility of the inclination is required; actual existence is not necessary.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Circular motion does not require an actual inclination, only its possibility, and straight and circular inclinations can coexist, as shown by a rolling sphere and a wheel.

The Daily Motion of the Outermost Sphere
Description of the outermost sphere's daily motion and its characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the characteristics of the daily motion of the outermost sphere, including its approximate completion, its designation as the first motion, and the concepts of celestial poles and equator.

Core Idea
The outermost sphere's daily motion is the first and most apparent, causing day and night.

This passage explains the foundational astronomical concept of the daily motion of the celestial sphere. It describes how this motion defines day and night, establishes the poles of the world, and introduces the celestial equator. Understanding these concepts is essential for grasping later discussions on the heavens and earth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the description of the daily motion and its approximate nature.
Notice that the passage uses precise language about the revolution being 'approximately' complete.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate terms like المحدد, الحركة اليومية, الحركة الأولى, قطب العالم, ومعدل النهار in the text.
These terms are central to the discussion.
3
Understand the relationship between the daily motion and the sun's motion
Note how the daily revolution is slightly less than a full day because of the sun's own eastward motion. This explains why the daily motion is approximate.
The passage gives a detailed explanation of this offset.
4
Explore the poles and equator
Recognize that every rotating sphere has two stationary poles and a zone of fastest motion. For the outermost sphere, these are the poles of the world and the celestial equator (معدل النهار).
The equator is defined as the great circle equidistant from the poles.
5
Reflect on the equator's significance
Consider the statement that at the equator, all planets rise and set, and there is no permanent visibility or invisibility. Also note how the celestial equator relates to the earth's equator.
The sun behaves differently; it is not at the zenith at the equator.

And among them is that it was said: He, i.e., the Encompasser (al-muhaddid) alone, is the one moving with the daily motion, a self-motion, and He is the mover of all the remaining spheres along with it, by way of subordination, in a day and its night a complete revolution approximately, not exactly, because its revolution completes before the completion of a day and its night by a short time. For the sun, when it is aligned with a part, and that part moves westward and the sun moves with its own motion eastward, when that part returns to its place, the revolution is completed, and the sun at that time has not by the motion of the whole returned to alignment with that place because it has traversed an arc eastward. So when the Encompasser rotates until the sun returns to its first position, then the day and its night are completed. And it is the greatest sphere encompassing all bodies because it defines the directions. And its fast daily motion is called the first motion, for it is observed first among the motions of the spheres because it is the most apparent, since through it are night and day and the rising and setting of the planets. Therefore it is not hidden from animals. And every sphere that moves in its place in a circle must have two stationary poles and a zone where its motion is fastest. Therefore he said: Its poles, i.e., the poles of this motion or sphere, are the poles of the world, because the corporeal world is the Encompasser and what is within it. And its zone, i.e., the greatest circle supposed in the middle of the poles such that its distance from them is equal, is called the equator of the day (mumadd al-nahar) for a reason you will learn in the investigations of the earth. And it, i.e., the zone called the equator, is where all planets have rising and setting, and there is nothing among them that is always visible or always hidden. It is always at the zenith, passing through it, and it is a complete small circle of the earth called the equator, as you will learn. Unlike the sun, for it is not at the zenith at the equator but rather inclines.

He said: 'And there is none among them' etc. is merely clarification of what preceded. He said: 'And it is a complete small circle' – the pronoun refers to where all planets have rising and setting, i.e., a complete small circle on the surface of the earth resulting from assuming the equator cutting the sphere of the world. He said: 'Because it traversed an arc eastward' – that arc in each day and night is less than one degree by fifty-two minutes and forty seconds, because they mentioned that the eccentric of the sun traverses by its own motion from west to east in each day and night fifty-nine minutes, eight seconds, and twenty thirds of the parts of the zodiacal circle, and its degrees will come in the book with the explanation of degree, minute, second, and third, God willing. He said: 'And it, i.e., the zone' etc. – 'it' is the subject, its predicate is 'is always at the zenith' etc., and 'where' indicates a location, and the pronoun in 'in it' refers to this location, and likewise his statement 'there' is an indication to this location, and likewise the pronoun 'it' in his statement 'and it is a revolution' refers to this location. He means that in this location there is a complete revolution of the earth parallel to the equator, and this revolution is called the equator, and the equator at this location is at the zenith of its people.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage describes the daily motion of the outermost celestial sphere (المحدد). It explains that this sphere moves approximately once per day, but the exact period is slightly less due to the sun's own motion eastward. This sphere is the greatest sphere, encompassing all bodies. Its daily motion is the first and most apparent motion, causing day and night. Every sphere rotating has two stationary poles and a zone (region) where motion is fastest. The poles of this sphere are the poles of the world. Its zone is the celestial equator (معدل النهار), which is the great circle equidistant from the poles. At this equator, all planets rise and set; no planet is always visible or always hidden. The celestial equator passes through the zenith of places on earth that lie on the earth's equator (خط الاستواء). The sun, however, is not at the zenith at the earth's equator but deviates. The marginal notes further clarify that the earth's equator is a small circle parallel to the celestial equator, and they also explain the calculation of the daily motion of the sun.

Misconception: The daily motion of the outermost sphere is exactly 24 hours.
Correction: The passage explains that the revolution is approximately a day and night, but slightly less due to the sun's own eastward motion. The exact period is slightly shorter than 24 hours.

Concentric and Eccentric Spheres: Definitions and Types
Defines concentric and eccentric spheres and their associated containing spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of concentric and eccentric spheres, and how they are classified into inclined and representative spheres, including Mercury's exception.

Core Idea
Celestial spheres are either concentric or eccentric, the latter embedded in other spheres.

This lesson opens the first beneficial point that provides the foundation for understanding planetary motion. We begin by defining the two fundamental types of celestial spheres—concentric and eccentric—and then see how the eccentric sphere is housed within another sphere, which is named according to context.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the definitions of the concentric sphere and the eccentric sphere. Notice that the concentric sphere shares its center with the earth, while the eccentric does not.
The terms 'muhaddib' (convex) and 'muqaʿʿar' (concave) refer to the two surfaces of the concentric sphere.
2
Identify the types of containing spheres
The passage explains that the eccentric sphere resides within the thickness of another sphere. For the moon, that sphere is called 'inclined' (al-māʾil); for other planets, it is called 'representative' (al-mumaththalāt). Note this distinction and Mercury's unique case of two eccentrics.
Remember: 'khārij' here means epicycle or eccentric sphere, not 'external'.
3
Pause to reflect on Mercury's exception
Mercury has two eccentrics: one in the representative sphere and one in the first eccentric. This is a preview of later details. For now, simply note that not all planets follow the same pattern.

(They are) not equal in thickness, but they take from thickness (which is) to the extent of the distance of its center from the center of the world, that thickness gradually (becoming thinner) until it ends at a point tangent to the epicycle) center from one of them (which is the containing completer at its convex) i.e., the convex of the epicycle (and from the other, which is the contained completer at its concave) i.e., the concave of the epicycle (alternating) in thickness and thinness. So the thickness of each of the two completers is opposite the thinness of the other, such that the volume of the sum of the (contained) interior within the epicycle and the (containing) exterior around it together is equal in all parts, because the thinness of one is compensated by the thickness of the other. And (in the middle of them) i.e., of the two completers (their volumes are equal), i.e., the volume of the middle of each is equal to the volume of the middle of the other, just as the thickness and thinness of each is equal to the thickness and thinness of the other. And (the concave of the interior) i.e., the contained, is parallel to the convex of the exterior i.e., the containing, and (their center) i.e., the center of the parallel concave and convex is one, which is the center of the world. And this is only valid if the epicycle is in the thickness of a concentric sphere. But if it is in the thickness of another epicycle, like one of Mercury's two epicycles, then the center of the two parallel surfaces is at that time the center of the other epicycle. And these
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is the first of two beneficial points intended to clarify the principles behind the varying planetary motions. The author begins by stating the purpose: to establish the relative positions of the fixed stars and to provide a foundation for understanding the differences in planetary motions. Then he defines two fundamental types of spheres: the concentric sphere (al-falak al-muwāfiq al-markaz), whose center coincides with the center of the earth (and thus the world), and the eccentric sphere (al-falak al-khārij al-markaz), whose center is offset. The eccentric sphere resides within the thickness of another sphere, called the 'inclined' (al-māʾil), in the case of the moon. For most other planets (except Mercury), the epicycles (kawārij) lie in concentric spheres called 'representative spheres' (al-mumaththalāt). Mercury has two epicycles. The author then explains that the containing sphere (the one in which the eccentric lies) is divided into two parts: the containing completer (al-mutammim al-ḥāwī) and the contained completer (al-mutammim al-muḥawā). These two parts vary in thickness: one is thick where the other is thin, and vice versa, such that the total volume of the two parts plus the eccentric is constant in all sections. The thickness gradually decreases from the point opposite the eccentric's center to a point tangent to it. The concave surface of the interior completer is parallel to the convex surface of the exterior completer, and their common center is the center of the world (when the eccentric lies in a concentric sphere). If the eccentric is itself within another eccentric (as with Mercury), the center shifts to the center of the outer eccentric. The passage ends abruptly, cutting off before completing the discussion.

The Two Completers and Their Properties
Explains division of containing sphere into completers and their geometric properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the containing sphere of an eccentric is divided into two completers with inversely varying thickness but equal total volume.

Core Idea
The two completers balance thickness and thinness to maintain uniform total volume.

Having learned about the eccentric sphere, we now examine the containing sphere that houses it. This sphere is divided into two parts called the completers, which have a unique property: their thickness varies inversely so that the overall volume remains constant. This lesson unpacks that concept.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division into completers
Read the passage to see how the containing sphere is split into two parts: one containing the eccentric and one contained by it. These are the completers (al-mutammimān).
The completers join with the eccentric to form a complete sphere.
2
Grasp the thickness variation
The completers are not equal in thickness. Their thickness gradually changes from thick to thin based on the offset of the eccentric's center. The passage describes this as 'taking from thickness to thinness' until a point tangent to the eccentric.
Think of it as one completer being thick where the other is thin, and vice versa.
3
Note the equality of volumes
Despite the variation, the total volume of the eccentric plus the two completers is the same in all sections. Also, the middle volumes of the two completers are equal. The concave and convex surfaces are parallel with a common center.
This property ensures the sphere is uniform overall.

Source Text

وأما إثبات أوضاعها بعضها من بعض في القرب والبعد والمحاذاة (ولنختم هذا البحث بفائدتين تنفعانك فيما ) سيأتيك ( بعد) من اختلاف حركات السيارات في الرؤية [عة؟] وبطئاً واستقامة ورجوعاً إذ لا بد لهذا الاختلاف من أصل يستند إليه . And as for establishing their positions relative to each other in nearness, distance, and alignment (and we will conclude this research with two benefits that will benefit you in what) will come after (you) regarding the differences in the motions of the planets in observation [عة?] and slowness, direct and retrograde, since this difference must have a principle to rely upon. (The first: the concentric sphere (al-falak al-muwāfiq al-markaz) is what its center is the center of the world, which is the center of the earth. And it (i.e., the concentric sphere) has two surfaces surrounding it from inside and outside; they are its convex (muhaddib) which is surrounding it from outside, and its concave (muqaʿʿar) which is opposite it. And) the eccentric sphere (al-falak al-khārij al-markaz) is a sphere surrounding the earth whose center is not the center of the earth, but it falls (i.e., its center inclines to a side of it, i.e., from the center of the earth). And (the eccentric sphere is situated in the thickness of another sphere, and that other sphere is called) the inclined (al-māʾil). This is only valid for the epicycle of the moon, for it is in the thickness of a concentric sphere called the inclined. As for the other planets except Mercury, their epicycles are in the thickness of concentric spheres called the representative spheres (al-mumaththalāt). As for Mercury, it has two epicycles, one in the thickness of the representative and the other in the thickness of the first epicycle as you will learn. And (that other sphere is divided) by virtue of the epicycle being in its thickness (into two parts): one containing the epicycle and the other surrounded by it, and they are called the completers (al-mutammimān) because by their joining with the epicycle the complete sphere of which that epicycle is a part is completed. (They are) not equal in thickness, but they take from thickness (which is) to the extent of the distance of its center from the center of the world, that thickness gradually (becoming thinner) until it ends at a point tangent to the epicycle) center from one of them (which is the containing completer at its convex) i.e., the convex of the epicycle (and from the other, which is the contained completer at its concave) i.e., the concave of the epicycle (alternating) in thickness and thinness. So the thickness of each of the two completers is opposite the thinness of the other, such that the volume of the sum of the (contained) interior within the epicycle and the (containing) exterior around it together is equal in all parts, because the thinness of one is compensated by the thickness of the other. And (in the middle of them) i.e., of the two completers (their volumes are equal), i.e., the volume of the middle of each is equal to the volume of the middle of the other, just as the thickness and thinness of each is equal to the thickness and thinness of the other. And (the concave of the interior) i.e., the contained, is parallel to the convex of the exterior i.e., the containing, and (their center) i.e., the center of the parallel concave and convex is one, which is the center of the world. And this is only valid if the epicycle is in the thickness of a concentric sphere. But if it is in the thickness of another epicycle, like one of Mercury's two epicycles, then the center of the two parallel surfaces is at that time the center of the other epicycle. And these
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is the first of two beneficial points intended to clarify the principles behind the varying planetary motions. The author begins by stating the purpose: to establish the relative positions of the fixed stars and to provide a foundation for understanding the differences in planetary motions. Then he defines two fundamental types of spheres: the concentric sphere (al-falak al-muwāfiq al-markaz), whose center coincides with the center of the earth (and thus the world), and the eccentric sphere (al-falak al-khārij al-markaz), whose center is offset. The eccentric sphere resides within the thickness of another sphere, called the 'inclined' (al-māʾil), in the case of the moon. For most other planets (except Mercury), the epicycles (kawārij) lie in concentric spheres called 'representative spheres' (al-mumaththalāt). Mercury has two epicycles. The author then explains that the containing sphere (the one in which the eccentric lies) is divided into two parts: the containing completer (al-mutammim al-ḥāwī) and the contained completer (al-mutammim al-muḥawā). These two parts vary in thickness: one is thick where the other is thin, and vice versa, such that the total volume of the two parts plus the eccentric is constant in all sections. The thickness gradually decreases from the point opposite the eccentric's center to a point tangent to it. The concave surface of the interior completer is parallel to the convex surface of the exterior completer, and their common center is the center of the world (when the eccentric lies in a concentric sphere). If the eccentric is itself within another eccentric (as with Mercury), the center shifts to the center of the outer eccentric. The passage ends abruptly, cutting off before completing the discussion.

Correction: The passage states they are not equal in thickness; rather, thickness varies inversely.

The passage says the thinness of one is compensated by the thickness of the other. In your spiritual life, where are you compensating for weakness in one area with strength in another? Are you relying on Allah's balance or your own?
?
The center of the parallel surfaces is the world's center, but only under certain conditions. Is the center of your life truly Allah, or are you shifting your center to other things like career or relationships, like the eccentric for Mercury? What would it take to recenter?

Three Necessary Consequences of Uniform Circular Motion and the Lunar Case
The author states the three necessary consequences of uniform circular motion and their partial negation in the moon's motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three necessary consequences of uniform circular motion and how the moon's motion contradicts two of them, leading to a refined model involving the equant.

Core Idea
Uniform circular motion forces three consequences; the moon breaks two.

In this passage, the author presents a deductive argument that any sphere moving uniformly on a circle must exhibit three specific properties. He then applies this to the moon's epicycle and shows that only one of these properties actually holds, leading to a revision of the model. This demonstrates the interplay between theoretical principles and empirical observation in medieval astronomy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note that the passage has a logical structure: a general rule, its application, and an observational contradiction.
2
Identify the three consequences
The author lists three things that necessarily follow from uniform circular motion. Find each one and understand its meaning.
These are: similarity of motion around the center, alignment of a diameter, and equality of distance.
3
Apply to the moon
See how the same three consequences are stated for the moon's epicycle. Notice the author says 'they require that...'.
The principles (الأصول) are the premises for the moon's motion.
4
Observe the deviation
The author says 'they found it contrary to that'. Identify which consequences are negated and which remain.
Only the equality of distance remains; the other two are negated.
5
Understand the logical conclusion
The negation of the necessary consequences entails the negation of the premises. Reflect on why this is a valid deductive step.
This is an example of modus tollens in a scientific context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يخفى على ذي فطرة سليمة أن كرة كالتدوير مثلا إذا تحركت على محيط دائرة كمنطقة الخارج حركة متشابهة على نهج واحد بلا تفاوت لزم هناك أمور ثلاثة. الأول: أن تكون حركة الكرة متشابهة حول مركز تلك الدائرة. الثاني: أن يكون قطر منها بعينه محاذياً لذلك المركز. الثالث: أن يتساوى بعد تلك الكرة عن مركز الدائرة. وحينئذ نقول: هذه الأصول التي قدروها في أفلاك القمر وحركاته يلزمها أن يكون القمر تشابه حركته أي حركة مركز تدويره حول مركز الخارج وأن يكون محاذاة قطر تدويره المار بالذروة والحضيض له أي لمركز الخارج أيضا. وأن يكون تساوي قربه وبعده أيضاً عند مركز الخارج دون مركز العالم. ثم إنهم وجدوه بخلافه فتشابه حركته أي حركة مركز تدويره حول مركز العالم والمحاذاة أي محاذاة قطر تدويره المار بالذروة والحضيض الأوسطين لنقطة من ذلك الخط المار بالمراكز والأوج والحضيض غير مركزهما أي مركز العالم والخارج، وتلك النقطة واقعة من جانب الأوج لتوسط مركز الخارج بينها وبين مركز العالم. والصواب أن يقال: هي من جانب الحضيض لتوسط مركز العالم بينها وبين النقط خارج المركز ومن حركة فلك آخر مائل أو تدوير أو جوزهر من غير أن يعلم بعضها أو كلها. مركز الخارج كما هو المشهور. وأما تساوي بعد مركز التدوير عن مركز الخارج فهو باق على حاله. وانتفاء اللازم الذي هو تشابه الحركة حول مركز الخارج ومحاذاة القطر المذكور له يوجب انتفاء الملزوم الذي هو الأصول التي ذكروها في القمر.
It is not hidden from one of sound instinct that if a sphere, such as an epicycle, moves uniformly on the circumference of a circle, such as the deferent, in a similar motion in one manner without variation, three things necessarily follow. First: that the motion of the sphere is similar around the center of that circle. Second: that a particular diameter of it is aligned with that center. Third: that the distance of that sphere from the center of the circle is equal. Then we say: These principles that they established for the spheres of the moon and its motions require that the moon (rather) that the motion of its epicycle center is similar around the center of the deferent, and that the alignment of its epicycle diameter passing through apogee and perigee is with that (i.e., with the center of the deferent) also, and that its nearness and farness are equal also at the center of the deferent, not at the center of the world. Then they found it contrary to that: so the similarity of its motion (i.e., the motion of its epicycle center) is around the center of the world, and the alignment (i.e., the alignment of its epicycle diameter passing through the mean apogee and perigee) is to a point on that line passing through the centers, apogee, and perigee, other than their centers (i.e., the center of the world and the deferent), and that point lies on the side of the apogee because the center of the deferent is between it and the center of the world. The correct view is to say: it is on the side of the perigee because the center of the world is between it and the point outside the center, and due to the motion of another inclined sphere, or an epicycle, or an evolute, without some or all of them being known. The center of the deferent is as is well-known. As for the equality of the distance of the epicycle center from the center of the deferent, it remains as it is. And the negation of the necessary consequence, which is the similarity of motion around the center of the deferent and the alignment of the mentioned diameter with it, entails the negation of the premisses, which are the principles that they stated for the moon.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a deductive argument establishing the principles for the moon's motion. The author first states a general rule: if a sphere moves uniformly on a circle, three consequences necessarily follow: (1) the sphere's motion is uniform around the center of the circle, (2) a specific diameter of the sphere always points toward that center, and (3) the sphere's distance from the center remains constant. Applying this to the moon, the authors of the original theory assumed that the moon's epicycle center moves uniformly around the center of the deferent (the 'center of the outside' or مركز الخارج), leading to these three consequences. However, observation shows the opposite: the first two consequences do not hold—the motion is uniform around the center of the world, not the deferent, and the alignment is to a point (the equant) other than the deferent's center. Only the third consequence (equal distance of the epicycle center from the deferent's center) remains true. Therefore, the original premises (الأصول) are negated because their necessary consequences are not fulfilled. The author then clarifies the correct description of the equant point and notes that the motion is explained by additional spheres (an inclined sphere, an epicycle, or an evolute). The commentator expands on this, explaining the conditions of the equant and the meaning of the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the premises are false, the conclusion is automatically false; but the author shows that only some consequences are negated, leading to a refined conclusion.
Correction: The author argues that because two necessary consequences are false, the original premises are invalidated—not that the conclusion (the observed motion) is false. The third consequence remains, and a more complex model is needed.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, which of the three consequences of uniform circular motion was found to be true for the moon?
A) The motion of the epicycle center is uniform around the deferent center.
B) The diameter of the epicycle is always aligned with the deferent center.
C) The distance of the epicycle center from the deferent center remains equal.
D) All three consequences were found to be false.

Key Takeaway
The moon's motion proves that not all consequences of uniform circular motion hold; only the equality of distance remains, necessitating a revision of the original premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows astronomers willing to abandon their premises when observation proves them false. What premises do I hold about my own faith or character that I have never tested against reality? Am I prepared to abandon them if they are untrue, even if they are comfortable?
?
The author corrects a common error about which side the equant point lies on, showing that even established views can be wrong. Where in my life have I uncritically accepted a commonly held belief without checking its foundation? Will I seek the truth even if it means being corrected?
?
Only one of the three consequences (equality of distance) survived scrutiny. This means the astronomers had to adjust their model while preserving what was true. What beliefs or practices in my life are sound and should be preserved, and what needs to be revised? Am I willing to undergo that refinement?

Objection: Multiple Causes for Same Effect
An objection is raised that observed states do not uniquely imply the proposed spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the deduction from observed states to specific spheres is challenged by the objection that multiple configurations can produce the same necessary conditions.

Core Idea
The same effect can have multiple causes — observation alone cannot prove a unique cause.

In the previous lesson, the author argued that the moon's observed states require a specific set of spheres. Now an objection is raised: the observed states are necessary conditions (اللازم), but they do not uniquely imply a single cause (الملزوم). This lesson explores the logical principle of 'sharing of necessary conditions' (اشتراك اللوازم) and why it undermines the inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the logical terms like اللازم, الملزوم, المساواة.
The passage includes both the objection and a commentator's note.
2
Identify the logical structure
Find the لازم (the observed states) and الملزوم (the spheres and motions). Understand that the objection calls into question the one-to-one correspondence between them.
3
Understand the objection about المساواة
The objection argues that the deduction is only valid if there is equality (المساواة) between لازم and ملزوم, which is not proven. Other configurations could produce the same states.
This is the core of the objection.
4
Consider the commentator's note
The commentator adds that the claim of no other configuration being observable is itself a matter of dispute.
This shows the objection is not easily dismissed.
5
Reflect on broader significance
Think about how this principle applies to scientific reasoning: empirical data often underdetermines theory.
This is a key lesson in the philosophy of science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه أورد على كلامهم اعتراضاً آخر فقال : كيف أي كيف يصح كلامهم وما ذكروه من أن القمر لما علم له بالرصد أحوال مخصوصة وجب أن يكون له أفلاك كذا وكذا متحركة على الوجوه المذكورة المقتضية لتحقق تلك الأحوال استدلال بوجود اللازم الذي هو تلك الأحوال على وجود الملزوم الذي هو تلك الأفلاك المتحركة على تلك الوجوه وإنما يصح هذا الاستدلال إذا علم المساواة بين اللازم والملزوم ولم تعلم المساواة هاهنا إذ يجوز أن يكون ثمة وضع آخر مغاير لما ذكروه يستلزم ذلك الوضع الآخر هذه الحركات المقتضية للأحوال المعلومة كما أن الوضع الذي بينوه يستلزمها أيضاً، لجواز اشتراك الأمور المختلفة في اللوازم وليس انتفاؤه أي انتفاء الوضع الآخر ضرورياً ولا مبرهناً عليه. قوله : ( ليس انتفاؤه ضرورياً ولا مبرهناً عليه . وأما قولهم لو كان هناك وضع آخر لعلمناه بالرصد فهو محل النزاع بعد .)
Then he raised another objection against their statement and said: 'How (i.e., how can their statement be correct) and what they mentioned, that when the moon is known through observation to have particular states, it must have such-and-such spheres moving in the mentioned ways that necessitate the realization of those states, is a deduction from the existence of the necessary condition (al-lāzim), which is those states, to the existence of the conditioned (al-malzūm), which is those spheres moving in those ways. This deduction is only valid if equality (al-musāwāt) between the necessary condition and the conditioned is known, but it is not known here, because it is possible that there is another configuration different from what they mentioned, which necessitates these motions that necessitate the known states, just as the configuration that they explained also necessitates them, due to the possibility of different things sharing the same necessary conditions. And its negation (i.e., the negation of the other configuration) is neither necessary nor demonstrated.' The commentator says: 'His statement: (its negation is neither necessary nor demonstrated). And as for their statement that if there were another configuration, we would have known it by observation, that is the point of dispute.'
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the deductive reasoning used in the previous lesson to infer the moon's spheres from observed states. The objection argues that the inference from the existence of a necessary condition (اللازم: the observed states) to the existence of a specific cause (الملزوم: the proposed spheres and motions) is valid only if there is a one-to-one correspondence (المساواة) between cause and effect. Since it is possible that multiple different configurations could produce the same observed states, the deduction is not conclusive. The commentator adds that the claim that any other configuration would have been detected by observation is itself a matter of dispute and not accepted by all.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we observe a set of states, we can deduce the exact cause.
Correction: Multiple different causes can produce the same effects, so observation alone does not guarantee a unique cause.

Checkpoint

A) The observed states are not reliable.
B) The necessary condition (اللازم) is not actually present.
C) The equality (المساواة) between the necessary condition and the conditioned is not established.
D) The spheres mentioned cannot possibly exist.

Key Takeaway
The deduction from observed states to specific spheres is invalid because multiple configurations can share the same necessary conditions.

How would it change your humility if you admitted that your inference might not be equal (musāwāt)?

The commentator notes that the claim that an alternative configuration would be detected by observation is disputed. What would it cost you to release that assumption and submit to the possibility that your knowledge is incomplete? Will you commit to examining one such assumption today?
