The Logical Condition for Causal Inference
An objection against inference from lunar states to spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an inference from an observed effect to a unique cause requires proof of exclusive causality, and how this principle is applied to critique the astronomers' argument about lunar spheres.

Core Idea
Observed effects do not prove a unique cause unless exclusivity is established.

This lesson examines a logical objection raised against the astronomers' claim that the moon's observed motions necessitate a specific arrangement of celestial spheres. The objection uses the fundamental kalam principle that inferring a cause from an effect requires establishing an exclusive connection between them. Without this, alternative explanations remain possible. We will explore the objection's structure and its implications for scientific reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the astronomers' inference and key terms
Read the passage and identify the inferred cause (الملزوم) and the observed effect (اللازم). Notice the condition for a valid inference: knowing the equivalence (المساواة).
The objection hinges on this condition.
2
Analyze why the equivalence is unknown
The passage states that it is possible for a different arrangement (وضع آخر) to produce the same effects. This is the core critique: multiple causes can share the same effect.
This is the principle of multiple realizability.
3
Evaluate the astronomers' response and its flaw
The commentators mention the astronomers' claim that other arrangements would be detectable by observation, but note that this is exactly the point in dispute. Recognize that this is a circular argument.
The passage ends with 'فهو محل النزاع بعد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه أورد على كلامهم اعتراضاً آخر فقال : ( كيف ) أي كيف يصح كلامهم (وما ذكروه ) من أن القمر لما علم له بالرصد أحوال مخصوصة وجب أن يكون له أفلاك كذا وكذا متحركة على الوجوه المذكورة المقتضية لتحقق تلك الأحوال ) استدلال بوجود اللازم الذي هو تلك الأحوال ( على وجود الملزوم ) الذي هو تلك الأفلاك المتحركة على تلك الوجوه ( وإنما يصح ) هذا الاستدلال ( إذا علم المساواة ) بين اللازم والملزوم ( ولم تعلم ) المساواة هاهنا ( إذ يجوز أن يكون ثمة وضع آخر مغاير لما ذكروه ( يستلزم) ذلك الوضع الآخر هذه الحركات) المقتضية للأحوال المعلومة كما أن الوضع الذي بينوه يستلزمها أيضاً، لجواز اشتراك الأمور المختلفة في اللوازم وليس انتفاؤه ) أي انتفاء الوضع الآخر ( ضرورياً ولا مبرهناً عليه .

قوله : (ليس انتفاؤه ضرورياً ولا مبرهنا عليه ) وأما قولهم لو كان هناك وضع آخر لعلمناه بالرصد فهو محل النزاع بعد .
Then he raised another objection against their statement, saying: (How) i.e., how can their statement be valid? (And what they mentioned) that the moon, since specific states were known for it by observation, it must have such-and-such spheres moving in the mentioned ways that necessitate the realization of those states—(is an) inference from the existence of the necessary concomitant, which is those states, (to the existence of the entailing thing), which is those spheres moving in those ways. (But this inference is valid only if the equivalence) between the necessary concomitant and the entailing thing (is known). (And the equivalence is not known) here, (for it is possible that there exists another arrangement different from what they mentioned, and that other arrangement necessitates these motions) that necessitate the known states, just as the arrangement they explained also necessitates them, because of the possibility of different things sharing the same necessary concomitants. And its non-existence—i.e., the non-existence of the other arrangement—(is neither necessary nor proven).

His statement: ('Its non-existence is neither necessary nor proven')—and as for their saying, 'If there were another arrangement, we would know it by observation,' that is the point of dispute.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the astronomers' argument that the observed motions of the moon prove the existence of a specific set of celestial spheres. The objection is a logical critique of the inference from effect to cause. The author begins by stating that the astronomers claim: since the moon has certain observed states (motions, positions), it must have such-and-such spheres moving in particular ways that produce those states. This is an inference from the observed effects (the 'necessary concomitant') to the proposed cause (the 'entailing thing'). The objection then points out that this inference is only valid if it is known that the necessary concomitant and the entailing thing are uniquely equivalent, i.e., that the same effect cannot be produced by any other cause. But here, such equivalence is not known, because it is possible that a completely different arrangement of spheres could still produce exactly the same observed motions. The objector argues that different causes can share the same effects. Therefore, the non-existence of an alternative arrangement is neither intuitively evident ('necessary') nor proven demonstrably ('proven'). The commentator then adds a marginal note: the astronomers' claim that if there were another arrangement we would know it by observation is itself the very point under dispute; it is not a valid refutation of the objection.

الملزوم
المساواة
الوضع
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an observed effect is known, the cause that produces it is uniquely determined.
Correction: The passage shows that multiple causes can produce the same effect, so inference requires proof of exclusivity (المساواة).

Checkpoint
What condition is required for a valid inference from a necessary concomitant (اللازم) to the entailing thing (الملزوم)?
A) The concomitant must be observed.
B) The equivalence (المساواة) between them must be known.
C) The entailing thing must be physically possible.
D) The concomitant must be unique to that entailing thing.

Key Takeaway
Inference from effect to cause is only logically valid if the effect is exclusive to that cause; otherwise, alternative explanations remain possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a particular outcome in your life must have a single cause, without considering alternative explanations? How might that assumption have led you to unjust judgments about yourself or others? Will you commit to pausing and asking 'Is this effect exclusive to this cause?' before drawing conclusions?
?
When you observe something in your life that seems to indicate a certain cause, do you instinctively leap to that conclusion or do you pause to consider other possibilities? What would it look like to apply the logical principle of checking for exclusivity in your daily decisions?
?
The astronomers claimed they would know any alternative arrangement by observation—yet the objector points out that very claim is disputed. Are there areas of your life where you are certain of a cause simply because you haven't seen an alternative? Could that certainty be misplaced? Will you seek to prove exclusivity rather than assume it?

Absolute Heavy and Center of Gravity
Author defines absolute heavy and distinguishes center of gravity from center of volume.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of absolute heavy and the difference between center of gravity and center of volume in kalam cosmology.

Core Idea
The center of gravity, not volume, must align with the world's center.

This passage from al-Mawaqif defines absolute heavy as that which seeks the center, but the author clarifies that it is the center of gravity, not the geometric center, that must coincide with the world's center. Understanding this distinction is crucial for later arguments about elemental motions and celestial spheres. The passage also addresses a potential objection and provides a clear example of homogeneous versus heterogeneous spheres.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of absolute heavy
Read the opening statement: 'and absolute heavy, which seeks the center, meaning that it requires the coincidence of its center of gravity with the center of the world.' Note that it specifies 'center of gravity'.
The definition is precise: it is about the center of gravity, not just any center.
2
Identify the two centers distinguished
The author distinguishes between center of volume (geometric center) and center of gravity (mass center). Understand each definition: center of volume is where all sides are equal; center of gravity is where the weight is balanced from all sides.
3
Analyze the example of heterogeneous sphere
Consider the example: a sphere half wood, half iron. In such a sphere, the center of volume is at the geometric center, but the center of gravity shifts toward the denser iron half. This shows the two centers can differ.
This example concretely illustrates the abstract distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ثقيل مطلق يطلب المركز على معنى أنه يقتضي انطباق مركز ثقله على مركز العالم عندنا
قوله: (فإن قيل إلخ) إن قرر هذا الاعتراض معارضة. كان الجواب المذكور بطريق المناقضة موجهاً وإن قرر بطريق المناقضة بأن يكون منعاً لكبرى المطوية. أعني قوله : كل ما تفني الرطوبات فهو يابس فلا توجيه للجواب فلا بد من إثبات المقدمة ولا يصح بأنه لم قلتم : أن النار البسيطة كذلك .
قوله : ( يقتضي انطباق مركز ثقله على مركز العالم لا على مركز حجمه فإنه لا يقتضي ذلك الانطباق وهو ظاهر مركز الحجم هو أن يكون من جميع الجوانب بالنسبة إليه على السوية، كما مر في بيان مركز الدوائر والكرات ويقتضيه كون ذلك العنصر كرة في وسط الكل كما سيذكره في المقصد الرابع ومركز الثقل هو أن يكون الثقل من جميع الجوانب على السوية بالنسبة إليه ويقتضيه كونه ثقيلاً مطلقاً وهما قد يتحدان كما إذا كانت الكرة متشابهة الأجزاء قد لا يتحدان كما في الكرة التي نصفها من خشب ونصفها من حديد .
And (3) absolute heavy, which seeks the center, meaning that it requires the coincidence of its center of gravity with the center of the world, according to us. His saying: (If it is said, etc.) – If this objection is formulated as a counterargument, then the mentioned answer by way of contradiction is appropriate. And if it is formulated as a contradiction by denying the major premise, namely his saying: "Everything that consumes moistures is dry," then there is no direction for the answer; rather, it is necessary to prove the premise, and it is not valid by saying: Why did you say that the simple fire is likewise? His saying: (requires the coincidence of its center of gravity with the center of the world, not with the center of its volume) – for it does not require that coincidence, and it is obvious. The center of volume is that from which all sides are equal relative to it, as mentioned in the explanation of the center of circles and spheres, and it is required by that element being a sphere in the middle of the whole, as he will mention in the fourth purpose. And the center of gravity is that from which gravity is equal from all sides relative to it, and it is required by its being absolutely heavy. They may coincide, as when the sphere has homogeneous parts, and they may not coincide, as in a sphere half of which is wood and half is iron.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines absolute heavy and explains the concept of center of gravity. The author states that absolute heavy requires its center of gravity to coincide with the center of the world. Then a commentary on an objection: if the objection is a denial of the major premise, then a different answer is needed. The author then clarifies that the requirement is for the center of gravity, not the center of volume. He distinguishes between center of volume (geometric center) and center of gravity (center of mass). They may coincide (homogeneous sphere) or differ (heterogeneous sphere).

Correction: The author explains that for heterogeneous objects, such as a sphere half wood and half iron, the center of gravity is not the geometric center of volume.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Definition of Mizaj
Defines mizaj and distinguishes it from mixing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mizaj as a similar intermediate quality resulting from element interaction, and distinguish it from the mixing process.

Core Idea
Mizaj is the final unified quality, not the process of mixing.

This lesson establishes the core definition of mizaj (mixture) as a key concept in Islamic natural philosophy. Before delving into debates about its nature, we must first understand what mizaj is and what it is not. The passage distinguishes between the resulting intermediate quality and the process that leads to it.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage that defines mizaj and its contrast with imtizaj/ikhtilat. Pay attention to the sequence: settling on one quality vs. the gathering that precedes it.
Note the terms 'مزاج' and 'امتزاج'.
2
Identify the key elements of the definition
The definition includes: a similar quality, result of interaction of minute elements in contact, and that the vehemence of each breaks the other's quality. List these components.
3
Distinguish mizaj from imtizaj
Compare the two terms: mizaj is the settled quality; imtizaj is the process of mixing. Why is this distinction important?
This distinction prevents confusion in further discussions.
4
Reflect on the role of 'similar' quality
Consider why the quality is called 'similar' (متشابهة). The next lesson will explain this further, but try to infer from the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا استقر الكل على كيفية واحدة متوسطة توسطاً ما بين الكيفيات الأربع فهذه الكيفية المتشابهة تسمى مزاجا. وما قبل ذلك الاجتماع المؤدي إلى الكيفية المذكورة يسمى امتزاجاً واختلاطاً لا مزاجاً. فحد المزاج بناء على ما تقرر بأنه كيفية متشابهة تحصل من تفاعل عناصر متصغرة الأجزاء المتماسة بحيث تكسر سورة كل منها سورة كيفية الآخر. قال الإمام الرازي: لا شبهة في أن الشيء لا يوصف بكونه مشابهاً لنفسه. وإنما قلنا للكيفية المزاجية إنها متشابهة لأن كل جزء من أجزاء المركب ممتاز بحقيقته عن الآخر فتكون الكيفية القائمة به غير الكيفية القائمة بالآخر. إلا أن تلك الكيفيات القائمة بتلك الأجزاء متساوية في النوع وهذا معنى تشابهها. وقال أيضاً: الكاسر ليس هو الكيفية لأن انكسار الكيفيتين المتضادتين إما معا أو على التعاقب، فإن حصل الانكساران معاً والعلة واجبة الحصول مع المعلول لزم أن تكون الكيفيتان الكاسرتان موجودتين على صرافتهما عند حصول انكساريهما وهو محال وإن كان انكسار إحداهما متقدماً على انكسار الأخرى لزم أن يعود المكسور المغلوب كاسراً غالباً. وهو أيضاً باطل فوجب أن يكون الكاسر هو الصورة التي هي مبادئ الكيفيات، وأما المنكسر فليس أيضاً الكيفية لأن الكيفية الواحدة بالذات لا يعرض لها الاشتداد والتنقص بل هما يعرضان لمحلها فالانكسار عبارة عن زوال الكيفيات الصرفة عن تلك البسائط. قوله: فحد المزاج بأنه كيفية متشابهة يمكن تطبيق هذا الحد على كل واحد من الاحتمالين المذكورين آنفا. لكن على الاحتمال الأول يتوجه على هذا الحد النقض بالمجموع المركب من اليبوسة والرطوبة المتوسطتين، أو من الحرارة والبرودة المتوسطتين أو من الثلاثة المتوسطة منها إذ يصدق على كل واحد من هذه المجموعات أنه كيفية متشابهة تحصل من تفاعل عناصر متصغرة الأجزاء مع أن المزاج عندهم هو الحقيقة الملتئمة من الكيفيات الأربع المتوسطة دون الملتئمة من الاثنين أو الثلاث.
When the whole settles upon one intermediate quality, an intermediate between the four qualities, this similar quality is called mizaj (mixture). And what precedes that gathering leading to the mentioned quality is called imtizaj (mixing) and ikhtilat (blending), not mizaj. The definition of mizaj, based on what has been established, is that it is a similar quality resulting from the interaction of elements with minute parts in contact, such that the vehemence of each of them breaks the vehemence of the quality of the other. Imam al-Razi said: There is no doubt that a thing is not described as being similar to itself. We only say of the qualitative mixture that it is similar because each part of the composite is distinguished in its reality from the other, so the quality subsisting in it is other than the quality subsisting in the other. But those qualities subsisting in those parts are equal in species, and this is the meaning of their similarity. He also said: The breaker is not the quality, because the breaking of two opposing qualities is either simultaneous or successive. If the two breakings occur simultaneously, and the cause is necessarily concomitant with the effect, then it would follow that the two breaking qualities exist in their purity at the time of their breakings, which is impossible. And if the breaking of one precedes the breaking of the other, then the broken and subdued would become a breaker and dominant, which is also false. Therefore, the breaker must be the form which is the principle of the qualities. As for the broken, it is also not the quality, because a single quality in itself does not undergo intensification and diminution; rather, these occur to its substrate. So breaking is the removal of the pure qualities from those simple bodies. He said: The definition of mizaj as a similar quality can be applied to each of the two possibilities mentioned earlier. However, on the first possibility, this definition is open to objection by the composite consisting of moderate dryness and moisture, or moderate heat and cold, or three moderate ones among them, for each of these combinations is a similar quality resulting from the interaction of elements with minute parts, while according to them, mizaj is the reality composed of the four moderate qualities, not the one composed of two or three.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of mizaj (mixture) and explains its nature, including a discussion by Imam al-Razi about the similarity of the resulting quality and the identity of the breaker (kasir). The passage begins by describing the process: when elements interact and settle on a single intermediate quality between the four primary qualities (heat, cold, dryness, wetness), that quality is called mizaj. The process leading to it is called imtizaj or ikhtilat, not mizaj itself. The formal definition is then given: mizaj is a similar quality produced from the interaction of elements with minute parts in contact, such that the vehemence of each element breaks the vehemence of the other's quality. Al-Razi then addresses the issue of similarity: a thing is not similar to itself, so how can the mixture's quality be called 'similar'? He explains that each part of the composite retains its own distinct reality, and the quality in each part is numerically distinct from the quality in another part, but they are equal in species (type), hence the overall quality is 'similar' in species. Then al-Razi argues about what causes the breaking of qualities. He argues that the breaker cannot be the quality itself because that would lead to contradictions: if two opposing qualities break each other simultaneously, they would both be present in their pure state at the moment of breaking, which is impossible; if one breaks first, the previously broken would become the breaker. Therefore, the breaker must be the form (sura) which is the principle of the qualities. The broken (the quality) is also not the quality itself, since a quality as such does not undergo intensification or diminution; these belong to the substrate. So breaking is the removal of pure qualities from the simple bodies. Finally, a marginal note criticizes the definition on the first possible interpretation: the definition would also apply to mixtures of only two or three moderate qualities, whereas the intended mizaj requires all four.

Key Takeaway
Mizaj is the settled intermediate quality, distinct from the mixing process, and is defined by mutual breaking of elemental vehemences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your inner state: do you feel like a chaotic mixture of desires and impulses (imtizaj), or have you settled into a coherent character (mizaj)? What one quality in you is too vehement and needs to be tempered by another?
?
The passage says the vehemence of each element breaks the other's vehemence. Where in your life do you need to allow a strong trait to be 'broken' by a complementary one to achieve balance? What would that look like starting tomorrow?

The Similarity of Mizaj and the Breaker
Explains similarity and identifies breaker as form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mixture's quality is called 'similar' and why the breaker of opposing qualities must be the form, not the quality itself.

Core Idea
The mixture's quality is similar in species, and its breaking is due to the substantial form.

Having defined mizaj, we now explore two deeper questions: why is the resulting quality described as 'similar' (mutashābiha)? And what causes the breaking (kasr) of the opposing qualities? Imam al-Razi provides arguments that clarify these points, and a final note critiques the definition's scope.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's explanation of similarity
Al-Razi argues that the mixture's quality is not similar to itself but that each part's quality is similar in species to the others. Read carefully and understand the distinction between numerical and specific identity.
2
Analyze the argument about the breaker
Al-Razi considers two possibilities for how opposing qualities break each other: simultaneously or successively. He rejects both if the breaker were the quality itself. Follow his reasoning.
Note the terms 'كاسر' (breaker) and 'صورة' (form).
3
Identify the conclusion about the breaker
Al-Razi concludes that the breaker is not the quality but the form. Understand why form is the principle of qualities.
4
Understand the critique of the definition
The final part notes that on one interpretation, the definition would include mixtures of only two or three moderate qualities, which are not true mizaj. This shows the precision required.

فإذا استقر الكل على كيفية واحدة متوسطة توسطاً ما بين الكيفيات الأربع فهذه الكيفية المتشابهة تسمى مزاجا. وما قبل ذلك الاجتماع المؤدي إلى الكيفية المذكورة يسمى امتزاجاً واختلاطاً لا مزاجاً. فحد المزاج بناء على ما تقرر بأنه كيفية متشابهة تحصل من تفاعل عناصر متصغرة الأجزاء المتماسة بحيث تكسر سورة كل منها سورة كيفية الآخر. قال الإمام الرازي: لا شبهة في أن الشيء لا يوصف بكونه مشابهاً لنفسه. وإنما قلنا للكيفية المزاجية إنها متشابهة لأن كل جزء من أجزاء المركب ممتاز بحقيقته عن الآخر فتكون الكيفية القائمة به غير الكيفية القائمة بالآخر. إلا أن تلك الكيفيات القائمة بتلك الأجزاء متساوية في النوع وهذا معنى تشابهها. وقال أيضاً: الكاسر ليس هو الكيفية لأن انكسار الكيفيتين المتضادتين إما معا أو على التعاقب، فإن حصل الانكساران معاً والعلة واجبة الحصول مع المعلول لزم أن تكون الكيفيتان الكاسرتان موجودتين على صرافتهما عند حصول انكساريهما وهو محال وإن كان انكسار إحداهما متقدماً على انكسار الأخرى لزم أن يعود المكسور المغلوب كاسراً غالباً. وهو أيضاً باطل فوجب أن يكون الكاسر هو الصورة التي هي مبادئ الكيفيات، وأما المنكسر فليس أيضاً الكيفية لأن الكيفية الواحدة بالذات لا يعرض لها الاشتداد والتنقص بل هما يعرضان لمحلها فالانكسار عبارة عن زوال الكيفيات الصرفة عن تلك البسائط. قوله: فحد المزاج بأنه كيفية متشابهة يمكن تطبيق هذا الحد على كل واحد من الاحتمالين المذكورين آنفا. لكن على الاحتمال الأول يتوجه على هذا الحد النقض بالمجموع المركب من اليبوسة والرطوبة المتوسطتين، أو من الحرارة والبرودة المتوسطتين أو من الثلاثة المتوسطة منها إذ يصدق على كل واحد من هذه المجموعات أنه كيفية متشابهة تحصل من تفاعل عناصر متصغرة الأجزاء مع أن المزاج عندهم هو الحقيقة الملتئمة من الكيفيات الأربع المتوسطة دون الملتئمة من الاثنين أو الثلاث.
When the whole settles upon one intermediate quality, an intermediate between the four qualities, this similar quality is called mizaj (mixture). And what precedes that gathering leading to the mentioned quality is called imtizaj (mixing) and ikhtilat (blending), not mizaj. The definition of mizaj, based on what has been established, is that it is a similar quality resulting from the interaction of elements with minute parts in contact, such that the vehemence of each of them breaks the vehemence of the quality of the other. Imam al-Razi said: There is no doubt that a thing is not described as being similar to itself. We only say of the qualitative mixture that it is similar because each part of the composite is distinguished in its reality from the other, so the quality subsisting in it is other than the quality subsisting in the other. But those qualities subsisting in those parts are equal in species, and this is the meaning of their similarity. He also said: The breaker is not the quality, because the breaking of two opposing qualities is either simultaneous or successive. If the two breakings occur simultaneously, and the cause is necessarily concomitant with the effect, then it would follow that the two breaking qualities exist in their purity at the time of their breakings, which is impossible. And if the breaking of one precedes the breaking of the other, then the broken and subdued would become a breaker and dominant, which is also false. Therefore, the breaker must be the form which is the principle of the qualities. As for the broken, it is also not the quality, because a single quality in itself does not undergo intensification and diminution; rather, these occur to its substrate. So breaking is the removal of the pure qualities from those simple bodies. He said: The definition of mizaj as a similar quality can be applied to each of the two possibilities mentioned earlier. However, on the first possibility, this definition is open to objection by the composite consisting of moderate dryness and moisture, or moderate heat and cold, or three moderate ones among them, for each of these combinations is a similar quality resulting from the interaction of elements with minute parts, while according to them, mizaj is the reality composed of the four moderate qualities, not the one composed of two or three.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of mizaj (mixture) and explains its nature, including a discussion by Imam al-Razi about the similarity of the resulting quality and the identity of the breaker (kasir). The passage begins by describing the process: when elements interact and settle on a single intermediate quality between the four primary qualities (heat, cold, dryness, wetness), that quality is called mizaj. The process leading to it is called imtizaj or ikhtilat, not mizaj itself. The formal definition is then given: mizaj is a similar quality produced from the interaction of elements with minute parts in contact, such that the vehemence of each element breaks the vehemence of the other's quality. Al-Razi then addresses the issue of similarity: a thing is not similar to itself, so how can the mixture's quality be called 'similar'? He explains that each part of the composite retains its own distinct reality, and the quality in each part is numerically distinct from the quality in another part, but they are equal in species (type), hence the overall quality is 'similar' in species. Then al-Razi argues about what causes the breaking of qualities. He argues that the breaker cannot be the quality itself because that would lead to contradictions: if two opposing qualities break each other simultaneously, they would both be present in their pure state at the moment of breaking, which is impossible; if one breaks first, the previously broken would become the breaker. Therefore, the breaker must be the form (sura) which is the principle of the qualities. The broken (the quality) is also not the quality itself, since a quality as such does not undergo intensification or diminution; these belong to the substrate. So breaking is the removal of pure qualities from the simple bodies. Finally, a marginal note criticizes the definition on the first possible interpretation: the definition would also apply to mixtures of only two or three moderate qualities, whereas the intended mizaj requires all four.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The breaker of opposing qualities is the quality itself.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the breaker cannot be the quality due to logical contradictions; it must be the substantial form that is the principle of qualities.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why cannot the breaker be the quality itself in the case of simultaneous breaking?
A) The two qualities would annihilate each other
B) The two breaking qualities would need to exist in purity at the same time, which is impossible
C) The cause must precede the effect
D) Qualities cannot interact

Key Takeaway
The mixture's quality is similar in species, and the breaking of opposing qualities is caused by the substantial form, not the qualities themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi argues that the breaker is not the quality itself but the underlying form. In your own spiritual life, do you often mistake the surface traits and habits (qualities) for your true self? Are you seeking to change your character by attacking qualities directly, or have you considered the foundational form—your very nature and intention before Allah—that generates those qualities? What would it mean for you to address the root of your actions?
?
The passage teaches that qualities do not intensify or diminish—only their substrates do. Think of a sin you struggle with repeatedly. Do you treat it as if it's a quality that simply waxes and wanes, or do you recognize that it's actually your own heart (the substrate) that is fluctuating? How can this realization reshape your repentance and self-accountability?
?
Al-Razi rejects both simultaneous and successive breaking, concluding that the form is the true breaker. Reflect on a conflict in your life where you are trying to 'break' a bad habit or opposing desire. Are you directing your efforts at the outward behavior (the quality) or at the deeper orientation of your soul (the form)? What practical step can you take today to focus on transforming your underlying disposition rather than just managing the symptoms?

Objection to the Proof from Disposition to Essence
Objection to the proof from disposition to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that difference in disposition may be due to accidents or individual identity rather than essence, and why this challenges the original proof.

Core Idea
Dispositions may attach to individual identity, not essence.

This passage presents a critical objection to the argument that differences in disposition necessarily indicate differences in essence. The objector questions the premise that all receptivities are essential concomitants, suggesting some may belong to individual identity (huwiyya) or accidental properties. This forces us to reconsider the strength of the inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to grasp the overall objection.
Pay attention to the key terms: qābiliyya, māhiyya, huwiyya.
2
Identify the objector's two claims
The objector denies two things: the proof itself and the exclusivity derived from 'receptivity is a concomitant of essence'. Underline these in the translation.
3
Understand the alternative explanation
The objector proposes that some receptivities are concomitants of huwiyya (individual identity) and that differences in disposition may be due to accidents (ʿawāriḍ). Reflect on how this undermines the original proof.
This is the core of the objection.
4
Note the grammatical remark
The second part explains that 'فإن قيل' refers to a counterargument, and 'قد صارت' is the predicate of 'كان'. This clarifies the structure of the original text.
This is a technical note for advanced readers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : دلیل اختلاف الماهية هذا ممنوع. وقوله : لأن القابلية من لوازم الماهية الحصر المستفاد منه ممنوع أيضاً. فإنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون بعض القابليات من لوازم الهوية دون الماهية وأن يكون اختلاف الاستعداد باعتبار العوارض دون الماهيات.
قوله : ( فإن قيل إشارة إلى المعارضة. وقوله : قد صارت خبر يكون.
His statement: 'The proof from difference of essence is inadmissible.' And his statement: 'Because receptivity (al-qābiliyya) is among the concomitants of essence (lawāzim al-māhiyya)' – the exclusivity (al-ḥaṣr) derived from it is also inadmissible. For why is it not permissible that some receptivities are among the concomitants of individual identity (huwiyya) rather than essence, and that difference of disposition is due to accidents (al-ʿawāriḍ) rather than essences? His statement: 'فإن قيل' refers to the objection. And his statement: 'قد صارت' is the predicate of 'كان'.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a critical objection to the proof that difference of disposition indicates difference of essence. The objector denies that receptivity is necessarily a concomitant of essence; some receptivities may be concomitant of the individual identity (al-huwiyya), which is the concrete individual's specific existence, not its universal essence. Therefore, different dispositions could be due to accidental properties rather than essential differences. This undermines the inference from observed differences in how a compound's parts respond to distillation to a difference in their essences. The second part of the passage explains that the phrase 'فإن قيل' (if it is said) refers to a counterargument, and 'قد صارت' is the predicate of the verb 'to be' (كان) in the earlier sentence. This is a technical grammatical note.

Key Terms
القابلية (al-qābiliyya)
الماهية (al-māhiyya)
الهوية (al-huwiyya)
العوارض (al-ʿawāriḍ)
الاستعداد (al-istiʿdād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objector rejects the entire argument outright, but the objection actually targets the specific premise that all receptivities are essential concomitants.
Correction: The objection grants that some receptivities may be essential but argues that others could be concomitant of huwiyya or accidents, thus the inference is not necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the objector, what could differences in disposition be due to instead of differences in essence?
A) The method of distillation
B) Individual identity or accidents
C) The logical form of the argument
D) The absence of any disposition

Key Takeaway
The objection weakens the proof from disposition to essence by showing that dispositions may attach to individual identity or accidents, not necessarily essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that someone's outward disposition (like their level of faith or character) must stem from their essential nature, only to later realize it was due to external circumstances? How does this lesson's objection challenge your tendency to make quick judgments about people's essences based on what you observe?
?
When you evaluate your own spiritual state, do you ever mistake temporary accidents (like a moment of weakness) for a permanent defect in your essence? How can the distinction between disposition (isti'dad) and identity (huwiyya) help you be more merciful with yourself?
?
What would it mean for your relationship with Allah if you accepted that some of your abilities to receive guidance (qabiliyya) are not fixed in your essence but can change due to your choices and efforts? Will you act on that today?

Interpreting 'Aali' and 'Tabi'i' in the Definition
Explains the two possible grammatical assignments of 'aali' and the meaning of 'tabi'i' as a descriptor of kamaal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the terms 'organic' and 'natural' in the definition of the vegetative soul require careful interpretation to exclude minerals and artificial perfections.

Core Idea
The definition of the vegetative soul hinges on precise grammatical and conceptual distinctions.

This passage from the Sharh al-Mawaqif delves into the nuances of the famous definition of the vegetative soul. The commentator clarifies two critical terms: 'aali' (organic) and 'tabi'i' (natural). He shows that 'organic' does not simply mean having heterogeneous parts—that would include minerals—but requires various faculties. Likewise, 'natural' is added to exclude artificial perfections. Understanding these refinements is essential for grasping the precise scope of the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overall sense of the discussion.
Pay attention to the technical terms being defined.
2
Identify the grammatical issues
Note the two possible grammatical assignments for 'aali' (as an attribute of 'body' or of 'perfection') and the reasoning for preferring one.
The commentator argues that making 'aali' an attribute of 'body' is 'more apparent' but raises issues with 'tabi'i' being separated.
3
Understand the meaning of 'organic'
The passage emphasizes that 'organic' does not merely mean having heterogeneous parts (which would include minerals), but having various powers like nutritive and growth.
This is a key conceptual refinement.
4
Note the distinction between natural and artificial
Some scholars take 'natural' as an attribute of 'perfection' to exclude perfections obtained through human craftsmanship.
Imam al-Razi is cited in support.
5
See how 'al-haythiyya' restricts the definition
The phrase 'in respect of being nourished and growing' specifies that the vegetative soul is a first perfection only from that consideration, excluding animal and human souls.
This final point ties the definition precisely to the vegetative soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : أي منوعها المشهور بينهم أن المجردات أمور بسيطة في الخارج . وإن كان بها فصول متنوعة لها في الذهن لكن المتبادر من الكمال الأول هاهنا ما هو المنوع الخارجي. قوله : ( وهذا أظهر لأن كون الجسم ذا آلة ولأن الموصوف حينئذ يكون أقرب في اللفظ لكنه لا يناسب أن يكون طبيعي مرفوعاً كما هو الأقرب . لأنه حينئذ يقع الفصل بالأجنبي بين الموصوف والصفة وقوله : ذا أجزاء متخالفة فقط. وإلا يلزم أن تكون المعادن البتة فإن لها أجزاء متخالفة مع أنها ليس بآلية إذ ليس لها إلا قوة حافظة. عن الكمال التقديرين فليس المراد بالآلي أن يكون الجسم ذا أجزاء متخالفة فقط بل وأن يكون أيضاً ذا قوى مختلفة كالغاذية والنامية ،وغيرهما، فإن آلات النفس بالذات هي ويتوسطها الأعضاء ومنهم من رفع طبيعي صفة للكمال احترازاً الصناعي فإن الكمال الأول قد يكون صناعيا يحصل بصنع الإنسان كما في السرير والصندوق، وقد يكون طبيعياً لا مدخل لصنعه فيه. قال الإمام الرازي: وقد جعل بعض المتأخرين الطبيعي صفة للكمال الأول هكذا النفس كمال أول طبيعي لجسم آلي وزعم أن الكمال الأول قد يكون طبيعياً كالقوى التي مبادئ الآثار وقد لا يكون كالتشكيلات الصناعية وهذا أقرب وبالحيثية يخرج كل كمال لا يلحق من هاتين الحيثتين. يعني أن قوله من حيث يتغذى وينمو يدل على أن النفس النباتية ليست كمالاً أولاً للجسم المذكور مطلقاً بل من الحيثية المذكورة فيخرج به الحد كل كمال لا يلحقه من هذه الحيثية كالنفس الحيوانية والإنسانية.
And his saying: i.e., its specific differencer. It is well-known among them that the immaterial entities are simple things in the external world, even if they have diverse differentiate in the mind, but what is understood from the first perfection here is the external specific differencer. His saying: 'And this is more apparent because the body having an instrument and because the described then would be closer in wording, but it is not suitable for 'natural' to be raised as is closer, because then a foreign separation occurs between the described and the attribute. And his saying: 'having only heterogeneous parts.' Otherwise minerals would necessarily be [organic], for they have heterogeneous parts despite not being organic since they only have a preservative force. [The discussion] about the perfection of the two readings is that what is meant by 'organic' is not that the body has only heterogeneous parts, but rather that it also has various powers like the nutritive, the growth, and others, for the instruments of the soul are essentially these and through them the limbs serve as intermediaries. And among them is one who raised 'natural' as an attribute of perfection as a precaution against the artificial, for the first perfection may be artificial, obtained by human craftsmanship as in the bed and the chest, and may be natural with no hand in its making. Imam al-Razi said: Some later scholars made 'natural' an attribute of the first perfection thus: the soul is a first natural perfection for an organic body. They claimed that the first perfection may be natural like the powers that are principles of effects, and may not be like industrial formations. This is closer. And by the consideration (al-haythiyya) is excluded every perfection that does not attach from these two considerations. Meaning, his saying 'in respect of being nourished and growing' indicates that the vegetative soul is not a first perfection for the mentioned body absolutely, but from the mentioned consideration. So the definition excludes every perfection that does not attach from this consideration, like the animal and human souls.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the definition of the vegetative soul. First, it clarifies that the 'specific differencer' (munawwi') of immaterial entities is taken as an external specific differencer, not merely a mental one. Then it discusses the two i'rab possibilities for 'aali', favoring the reading where it is an attribute of 'body' because it is closer in wording and avoids separating 'natural' from its described term. However, it notes the downside that if 'natural' is raised (as an attribute of perfection) it is problematic because then a foreign element separates the attribute from the described. Next, it explains that 'organic' does not merely mean having heterogeneous parts (which would incorrectly include minerals), but having various faculties (like nutritive and growth) which serve as instruments. The passage then notes that some take 'natural' as an attribute of 'perfection' to exclude artificial perfections. Al-Razi is cited in support of this view. Finally, it explains the phrase 'in respect of being nourished and growing' which restricts the definition to the vegetative soul, excluding other perfections like animal and human souls that do not share that respect.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'organic' simply means having heterogeneous parts.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'organic' requires having distinct faculties (nutritive, growth) that act as instruments, not merely heterogeneous parts. Otherwise minerals would be included.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason for rejecting the view that 'organic' means only having heterogeneous parts?
A) It would include minerals, which are not considered organic
B) It contradicts the opinion of Imam al-Razi
C) It is not grammatically possible
D) It would exclude too many things

Key Takeaway
The definition of the vegetative soul is precise: it is a first natural perfection for an organic body, where 'organic' implies having various faculties, and 'natural' excludes artificial perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author distinguishes between natural and artificial perfections. Consider your own spiritual practices: are they 'natural'—flowing from your genuine disposition—or 'artificial'—performed out of habit or social expectation without inner life? Which of your actions would the Imam consider 'first perfections' of your soul?
?
The vegetative soul is defined 'from the consideration of being nourished and growing'. Reflect on one spiritual nourishment you have been neglecting. How would your soul's growth change if you fed it with that nourishment daily this week?

The Definition of the Soul and Meaning of 'Possessing Life Potentially'
The author explains the definition of the soul and distinguishes celestial from other souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the soul as 'first perfection of a natural organic body' and the meaning of the phrase 'possessing life potentially' that distinguishes celestial souls from other souls.

Core Idea
The soul is defined by its potential to produce life activities, but not always actually.

This lesson clarifies the philosophical definition of the soul used in Islamic theology. The author distinguishes celestial souls, whose life activities are always actual, from animal, human, and plant souls, whose activities may be potential. We will explore the key phrase 'possessing life potentially' and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the careful distinctions the author makes.
2
Identify the two views on celestial souls
The passage mentions two schools: one assigns souls to all spheres, another only to universal spheres. Understand how the definition applies in both.
3
Distinguish actual vs. potential activities
Celestial souls always produce life activities actually, while other souls do so potentially (i.e., not always). Mark this contrast.
4
Examine al-Razi's interpretation
Imam al-Razi explains that the soul's potential includes growth, nourishment, and sometimes sensation and movement. See how this aligns with the definition.
5
Reflect on 'possessing life potentially'
Consider why the phrase is crucial to exclude celestial souls and include others. Write one sentence summarizing its meaning
This links to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلك من المرصد الأول - الفصل الثالث : المقدمة في تعريف النفس الأفلاك نفساً . وأما على رأى من ذهب إلى أن النفوس للأفلاك الكلية فقط والأفلاك الجزئية كالخارج والتدوير بمنزلة آلات لها فلا تخرج به فاحتيج إلى القيد الأخير لتخرج عن التعريف على المذهبين وذلك لأن النفوس الفلكية وإن كانت كمالات أولية أجسام طبيعية آلية لكنها ليس يصدر عنها أفاعيل الحياة بالقوة بل يصدر عنها ما يصدر من أفاعيل الحياة كالحركة الإرادية مثلا دائما بخلاف النفوس الحيوانية، فإن أفعالها قد تكون بالقوة إذ ليس لحيوان في التغذية والتنمية وتوليد المثل والإدراك والحركة دائماً بل قد يكون كل واحد من هذه الأفعال فيه بالقوة وكذا حال النفس الإنسانية بالقياس إلى تعقل الكليات والاستنباط بالآراء وحال النفس النباتية بالنسبة إلى ما يصدر عنها. فمعنى قوله : ذي حياة أنه يصدر عنه بعض أفاعيل الحياة ومعنى قوله بالقوة أن ذلك الصدور لا يكون بالفعل دائماً. وفسرها الإمام الرازي بقوله : أي من شأنه أن يحيا بالنشوء ويبقى بالغذاء وربما يحيا بالإحساس والتحريك .
From the first observatory – the third chapter: introduction to the definition of the soul: the celestial spheres have souls. As for the opinion of one who holds that the souls belong only to the universal spheres and that the partial spheres like the epicycle and the deferent are like instruments for them, they are not excluded by it; so the last restriction was needed to exclude them from the definition according to the two schools. That is because the celestial souls, although they are first perfections of natural organic bodies, yet the activities of life do not proceed from them potentially; rather, what proceeds from them of the activities of life, such as voluntary motion, proceeds always actually, unlike the animal souls, for their actions may be potential, since an animal does not constantly have feeding, growth, reproduction, perception, and motion; rather, each one of these actions may be potential in it. Likewise the human soul with respect to intellection of universals and deduction by opinions, and the plant soul with respect to what proceeds from it. So the meaning of His saying (possessing life) is that some activities of life proceed from it, and the meaning of His saying (potentially) is that this proceeding is not always actual. And Imam al-Razi interpreted it by his saying: meaning that it is its nature to live by growth and to subsist by nourishment, and sometimes it lives by sensation and movement.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage defines the soul and explains the key phrase 'possessing life potentially'. The author begins by situating the discussion within the context of celestial and other souls. He notes that according to one view, only the universal spheres have souls, while the partial spheres are like instruments. The definition of the soul as 'first perfection of a natural organic body' applies to celestial souls, but their activities of life are always actual, not potential. In contrast, animal, human, and plant souls have potential activities. The meaning of 'possessing life' is that some life activities proceed from it, and 'potentially' means that this proceeding is not constant. Al-Razi adds that the soul's nature includes growth, nourishment, and sometimes sensation and motion. This clarifies the distinctive characteristic of the soul across different beings.

Key Terms
النفس (soul)
الكمال (perfection)
القوة (potentiality)
الحياة (life)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'potentiality' means the soul is not active at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'potential' means the activities of life are not always actual, not that the soul is inactive. For example, animal souls do produce actions like feeding, but not perpetually.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes celestial souls from animal souls?
A) Celestial souls have no potentiality at all
B) Celestial souls produce life activities always actually, while animal souls do so potentially
C) Celestial souls are not 'first perfections'
D) Animal souls have no activities at all

Key Takeaway
The soul is defined as a first perfection of a natural organic body, with 'possessing life potentially' meaning that life activities proceed from it but not always actually, except for celestial souls which are always actual.

The passage distinguishes between souls that are always actual and those that are potential. Look at your own soul right now—are you living in the actuality of faith or just its potential? What would it mean to live out your faith 'actually' today?

Proof of Non-Corporeal Principles and the Best Definition of the Soul
Argument for non-corporeal principles and superiority of defining soul as perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for non-corporeal principles in living bodies and why the definition of the soul as 'perfection' is superior to 'form' and 'power'.

Core Idea
The soul is a non-corporeal principle best defined as 'perfection' because it encompasses all its aspects.

The author begins by establishing that the effects we observe in living beings—sensation, motion, nutrition—cannot be explained by corporeality alone, since all bodies share corporeality but not all produce these effects. Therefore, there must be non-corporeal principles, called the soul. He then evaluates three possible definitions of the soul—power, form, and perfection—and argues that 'perfection' is the best because it captures the soul's relation to the species and avoids the ambiguities of the other terms.

Guided Path
1
Observe the argument from effects
Read the opening lines where the author presents the empirical observation that effects like sensation and motion are not due to corporeality; they require non-corporeal principles.
This is a key inference in kalam.
2
Identify the three aspects of the soul
Notice that the soul is considered under three aspects: as principle (power), as related to matter (form), and as related to genus (perfection). The author will argue which is best.
3
Understand the superiority of 'perfection' over 'form'
The rational soul is immaterial, so 'form' applies only metaphorically. 'Perfection' indicates the species and implicitly matter, while 'form' indicates matter alone.
4
Understand the superiority of 'perfection' over 'power'
'Power' is homonymous between active and passive, creating ambiguity. 'Perfection' covers both without confusion and defines the soul from all its aspects.

( ولأن القوة اسم لها) أي للنفس (من حيث هي مبدأ الآثار وهو بعض جهاته) أي جهات هذا المعرف فتعرفه من هذه الجهة فقط ، ( والكمال اسم لها من حيث يتم بها الحقيقة) النوعية المستتبعة لآثارها ( فتعرفها من جميع جهاته. ولا ريب في أن تعريف الشيء بجميع جهاته أولى من تعريفه ببعضها . قوله : ( يصدر عنها آثار إلخ الظاهر أن هذا الكلام يشعر أن جميع تلك الآثار صادرة عن تلك الأجسام. وكذا قوله : فهي لمبادئ في تلك الأجسام مشعر بظاهره أن جميع تلك الآثار لها مبادئ في الأجسام المذكورة مع أن بعض الآثار كالإدراكات العقلية إنما تصدر عندهم من المبدأ الفياض فيكون هو مبدأ لها. ويمكن أن يقال لعل المراد بمصدرية الشيء ومبدئية لها ما هو أعم من كونه فاعلا لها أو محلا لها وظاهر أن النفس الإنسانية محل لإدراكاتهما وإن لم تكن فاعلة لها، أو يقال : إن النفس الإنسانية بحركاتها الاختيارية في المعقولات تكون منشأ لاستعدادات موجبة لتلك الإدراكات فبهذا الاعتبار تجعل هي مبدأ لها . قوله : ( ولا شك أن وضع المنسوب) أي وضع الكمال مثلاً المنسوب إلى ما هو أقرب أي إلى النوع الذي هو أقرب إلى الجنس مكانه أي مكان الجنس أولى من وضع المنسوب أي من وضع الصورة مثلا المنسوبة إلى ما ليس أقرب أي إلى المادة التي ليست أقرب إلى الجنس كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( إذ لا دلالة حينئذ على النوع وذلك لأنه المتبادر من إطلاق الصورة هو الصورة الجسمية على ما هو الكثير الشائع أو مطلق الصورة المتناول للصورتين أي النوعية والجسمية وأياً ما كان فلا دلالة حينئذ للنوع، ولا يتبادر الصورة النوعية من إطلاق اللفظ حتى يتصور هناك دلالة على النوع. وقوله : وللنفس قوة الإدراك وهو مثل القوة النظرية للنفس الإنسانية. وقوله : فتعرفه أي فتعرف القوة. وقوله : اسم لها أي للنفس وقوله فيعرفه أي يعرف الكمال هذا المعرف .
Notes containing benefits by which the goal is realized in this place. The first: We observe that effects proceed from them not in a single manner, as we mentioned of sensation, motion, nutrition, growth, and reproduction. And that proceeding is not due to the corporeality common to all bodies, because those effects are absent from other bodies that share in corporeality. So they (the effects) have principles in those bodies other than their corporeality, and these principles are not bodies; otherwise the argument would recur in them. Rather, they are powers attached to bodies. And it is called soul. So the soul has three aspects and names according to them: insofar as it is a principle of the mentioned effects, it is called power; relative to the matter that bears it, it is called form; relative to the nature of the genus by which it is specified and perfected, it is called perfection. And defining the soul as perfection is better than as form, because the form is the one that inheres in matter, but the rational soul is not like that, since it is immaterial, so the name form does not apply to it except metaphorically, in that it is attached to the body and its possibility subsists before its existence. Yet despite its immateriality in itself, it is a perfection for the body, just as kingship is a perfection for the city in terms of administration and disposal even if not present in it. And because perfection is measured relative to the species, which is closer to the nature of the genus (due to the validity of predication between them) than matter, relative to which form is measured, since there is no predication between them. There is no doubt that placing the relative to what is closer to the genus in its place is better than placing the relative to what is not closer. How could defining it as perfection not be better, when matter is included in the species without the converse? So if one indicates the species by perfection, one implicitly indicates matter, unlike indicating matter by form, for there is no indication of the species then. Thus the first indication is more complete than the second. Likewise, defining the soul as perfection is better than as power, because power is for passivity, and active power is not synonymous. For the word power is homonymous for two meanings: active power and passive power. The soul has the power of perception, which is passive, and the power of motion, which is active. It is not preferable to consider one over the other, nor is it permissible to consider them together, for that would spoil the definition. But the word perfection encompasses them with one meaning, so there is no problem. And because power is a name for it insofar as it is a principle of effects, which is one of its aspects, so it defines it from that aspect only. Perfection is a name for it insofar as the specific reality that entails its effects is perfected by it, so it defines it from all its aspects. There is no doubt that defining a thing from all its aspects is better than defining it from some of them. His statement: 'Effects proceed from them, etc.' Apparently this statement suggests that all those effects proceed from those bodies. Likewise his statement: 'So they have principles in those bodies' apparently suggests that all those effects have principles in the mentioned bodies, although some effects, such as intellectual perceptions, according to them proceed only from the Active Intellect, so He is their principle. It can be said: perhaps what is meant by a thing being a source or principle of something is more general than being its agent or its locus. It is apparent that the human soul is a locus for its perceptions even if it is not their agent. Or it is said: The human soul, by its voluntary movements in intelligibles, becomes a source of dispositions necessitating those perceptions, so in this respect it is considered their principle. His statement: 'There is no doubt that placing the relative' i.e., placing perfection, for example, relative to what is closer, i.e., to the species which is closer to the genus, in its place i.e., the place of the genus, is better than placing the relative i.e., placing form, for example, relative to what is not closer, i.e., to matter which is not closer to the genus, as is not hidden. His statement: 'For there is no indication of the species then' and that is because the immediate understanding from the absolute use of form is the corporeal form, according to common usage, or the absolute form encompassing both forms, i.e., the specific and the corporeal. In either case, there is no indication of the species then, and the specific form is not understood from the absolute use of the word such that indication of the species could be conceived. And his statement: 'And the soul has the power of perception' which is like the theoretical power of the human soul. And his statement: 'So it defines it' i.e., it defines the power. And his statement: 'a name for it' i.e., for the soul. And his statement: 'so it defines it' i.e., defines perfection, this definiendum.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage contains the first note (تنبيه) which argues that the observed effects in living bodies require principles that are not corporeal. The author states that effects like sensation and motion are not due to corporeality alone because they are absent in bodies that share the same corporeality. Therefore, they must have principles (مبادئ) that are not bodies but powers (قوى) attached to bodies. These principles are called the soul. Then the author discusses three aspects of the soul: as a principle of effects it is called 'power' (قوة), relative to matter it is called 'form' (صورة), and relative to the genus it is called 'perfection' (كمال). He argues that defining the soul as 'perfection' is superior to defining it as 'form' or 'power'. The argument against 'form' is that the rational soul is immaterial and does not inhere like a form; it is only metaphorically a form. Moreover, 'perfection' indicates the species and implicitly matter, while 'form' indicates matter only. Against 'power', the word 'power' is homonymous between active and passive, and considering either alone or both together is problematic, whereas 'perfection' encompasses both. Finally, he includes marginal comments that address an objection: some effects like intellectual perceptions come from the Active Intellect, not the soul. The answer is that the soul can be a locus or a source of dispositions, thus it is a principle in a broader sense. Additional marginalia clarify points in the superiority argument.

Key Terms
مبدأ (principle)
قوة (power)
صورة (form)
كمال (perfection)
العقل الفعال (Active Intellect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All effects attributed to the soul are directly produced by the soul itself.
Correction: Some effects, like intellectual perceptions, come from the Active Intellect; the soul is a locus or a source of dispositions, not necessarily the agent.

Checkpoint
Why is defining the soul as 'perfection' better than defining it as 'power'?
A) Because 'power' is homonymous and 'perfection' has a single meaning encompassing both active and passive powers
B) Because 'perfection' is a more elevated term
C) Because 'power' only applies to the body
D) Because 'perfection' is the term used in the Quran

Key Takeaway
The soul is defined most comprehensively as 'perfection' because it captures its role in completing the species while avoiding the ambiguities of 'form' and 'power'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The soul is a perfection for the body, just as kingship is for a city. If your soul is the king of your body, are you governing your desires and limbs with wisdom and justice, or are they ruling you? What specific change can you make today to assert your soul's authority?

The Soul's Names: Attachment and Separation
The author explains the naming of the soul and intellect based on relation to the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the soul is called 'soul' only when attached to the body and 'intellect' when separate.

Core Idea
The soul and intellect are one substance named by relation.

This short note clarifies a subtle but crucial point: the same entity can have different names depending on its state. The soul is not a separate substance from the intellect; they are one, but we name it 'soul' due to its attachment to the body and 'intellect' when it becomes independent. This helps avoid confusion about the nature of the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the key terms: تبرؤ (separation), تعلق (attachment), عقل (intellect), اشتراك لفظي (homonymy).
2
Understand the main claim
The author states that the soul can separate from the body and exist immaterially. However, the term 'soul' is only used because of its attachment to the body. When separated, the proper name is 'intellect'.
3
Grasp the principle of naming
The passage explains a general rule: a thing has one name based on its essence, and another based on its relation to something else. Apply this to the soul-intellect case.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some think the soul and intellect are different substances. The passage corrects this: they are the same reality, named differently according to context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه الثاني : النفس في بعض الأشياء كالإنسان ( قد تتبرأ عن البدن ) بأن تكون مجردة غير حالة فيه ( لكن لا يتناوله اسم النفس إلا باعتبار تعلقها به ) حتى إذا انقطع ذلك التعلق أو قطع النظر عنه لم يتناوله اسم النفس إلا باشتراك اللفظ بل الاسم الخاص بها حينئذ هو العقل، ( وقد يكون للشيء باعتبار ذاته وجوهره (اسم، وباعتبار تعلقه ) وإضافته إلى غيره (اسم
The second note: The soul in some things, like man, may separate from the body, being immaterial and not subsisting in it. But the name 'soul' applies to it only in consideration of its attachment to the body. So when that attachment is severed or disregarded, the name 'soul' applies to it only by homonymy; rather, its specific name then is 'intellect'. And sometimes a thing has a name in consideration of its essence and substance, and a name in consideration of its attachment and relation to another.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is the second note (تنبيه ثاني) which addresses the soul's relation to the body in terms of naming. The author states that in some beings, like humans, the soul can separate from the body, being immaterial and not inhering in it. However, the term 'soul' (نفس) is only applied to it due to its attachment to the body. Once that attachment is severed or disregarded, the term 'soul' applies only by homonymy (اشتراك لفظي), meaning the same word is used but with a different meaning. The proper name for the separate entity is 'intellect' (عقل). The author adds a general principle: a thing may have one name based on its essence and substance, and another name based on its relation to something else. This clarifies that the soul and intellect are the same entity but named differently according to its state of attachment or detachment.

التبرؤ
التعلق
العقل
الاشتراك اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the soul and intellect are two different substances with separate essences.
Correction: They are one and the same substance; the difference is only in naming based on its relation to the body (attachment vs. detachment).

Key Takeaway

Critique of the Philosophers' Proof for Multiple Faculties
Critique of philosophers' principle as basis for multiple faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-fold critique of the philosophers' argument that multiple actions require multiple faculties, and how the possibility of multiple instruments allows a single faculty to produce diverse effects.

Core Idea
Multiple actions do not prove multiple faculties; different instruments can explain diversity.

Philosophers held that a single entity cannot produce a variety of effects, so different actions require distinct faculties. Sānūsī here challenges this foundational principle and shows that even if it were true, the presence of multiple instruments undermines the conclusion. This passage demonstrates how theological critique engages with philosophical premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the two-part structure: first attacking the principle itself, then conceding for argument's sake.
2
Identify the philosophers' principle
Find the statement 'the one cannot issue from one except one' (الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد). Understand how this leads to multiple faculties.
3
Understand the first critique
The author states that this principle has already been shown to be weak in previous discussions. This is a foundational attack.
The author does not repeat the proof of weakness here; it is assumed.
4
Understand the second critique
Even if the principle is accepted, its application requires that there be no multiplicity of instruments. But in the body there are many instruments, so a single faculty can produce multiple actions via different instruments.
5
Grasp the final conclusion
The author concludes that the multiplicity of faculties is merely conceptual (بالاعتبار), not real. One faculty can perform all actions using different tools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : إثبات تعدد القوى وتغايرها بالذوات على رأي الحكماء ( بناء ) أي مبني على أصلهم إلى من أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد، ولا جاز أن يستند ( (الكل) أي جمع الأفعال المذكورة ) إلى قوة واحدة ) بالذات (وقد ثبت ) فيما مر ( ضعفه ) أي ضعف هذا الأصل وفساده فلا يصح بني عليه من تعدد القوى وتغايرها ( ثم) إن سلمنا صحته قلنا : شرطه عدم تعديد الآلات والقوابل) ومع يجوز أن يصدر عن الواحد أشياء متكثرة اتفاقاً ( وأنه ) أي عدم تعدد الآلة والقابل فيما نحن بصدده ( غير معلوم ) فجاز حينئذ أن لا يكون هناك إلاقوة واحدة تجذب الطعام بآلة وتمسكه بأخرى وتهضمه بثالثة وتدفع الفضل بآلة رابعة وتورد الغذاء تارةً أكثر من المتحلل وتارة أنقص أو مساوياً فلا تعدد في هذه القوى إلا بالاعتبار.
Warning: The proof of the multiplicity of faculties and their distinction in essence according to the philosophers is built upon their principle that 'the one does not issue from one except one.' It is not permissible that the whole, i.e., all the mentioned actions, be attributed to a single faculty in essence. And it has been established in what preceded that this principle is weak and unsound, so the multiplicity and distinction of faculties cannot be built upon it. Then, if we concede its soundness, we say: its condition is the non-multiplicity of instruments and receptacles. And it is possible for multiple things to issue from the one accidentally. And that, i.e., the non-multiplicity of instrument and receptacle in our case, is not known. So then it is possible that there is only one faculty that attracts food with one instrument, retains it with another, digests it with a third, expels waste with a fourth, and supplies food sometimes more than the decomposed amount, sometimes less or equal; so there is no multiplicity in these faculties except by consideration.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the philosophers' argument for the multiplicity of faculties. The philosophers base their proof on the principle that 'the one cannot issue from one except one' (الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد). Since various actions (attracting, retaining, digesting, expelling) are observed, they conclude each requires a distinct faculty. The author first notes that this principle has already been proven weak earlier. Even if accepted, the principle requires that there be only one instrument and receptacle for the agent; but in the body there are multiple instruments (different organs and parts). Therefore, a single faculty could produce multiple actions using different instruments. Moreover, it is unknown that there is only one instrument. Thus, the multiplicity is merely conceptual, not real.

Key Terms
تعدد القوى
الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا واحد
الآلات
القوابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple actions necessarily imply multiple faculties or agents.
Correction: Different instruments can allow a single faculty to produce multiple effects, so multiplicity of actions does not prove multiplicity of faculties.

Checkpoint
What condition does the author require for the principle 'the one cannot issue from one except one' to imply multiple faculties?
A) Multiple instruments and receptacles
B) Non-multiplicity of instruments and receptacles
C) Multiple agents
D) Divine intervention

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof for multiple faculties fails because their principle is unsound and, even if sound, does not apply due to the multiplicity of instruments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You assume that because you have many desires and impulses, you have multiple 'selves' fighting inside you. But this lesson teaches that one soul can produce multiple actions through different instruments. Are you using that as an excuse to avoid taking responsibility for your actions, attributing your sins to a 'bad self' rather than facing that you alone chose them? This week, catch yourself making that excuse and instead say: 'I am one person; I own all my actions. Only I can change them.'
?
The philosophers accepted a principle without question, and it led them to a false conclusion. What principle have you accepted in your life—from culture, family, or your own assumptions—that you have never tested against the Qur’an and Sunnah? Consider one such principle today, write it down, and seek evidence for or against it. Do not rest until you know whether it is true or false.
?
When you fail in worship, do you blame your 'lack of focus' or 'weak will' as separate faculties that control you? The author says a single faculty can have different actions depending on the instrument. Your heart is one faculty, but its action depends on which 'instrument' you feed it. Are you feeding your heart with the right instruments? List three 'instruments' you give your heart daily—what you watch, listen to, say—and decide to change one of them tomorrow to strengthen your worship.

The Service of the Four Qualities to the Natural Powers
Al-Jurjani explains how qualities serve natural powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how heat, cold, dryness, and moisture assist the attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive powers of the plant soul.

Core Idea
Each of the four qualities serves a specific set of natural powers, sometimes indirectly.

This passage from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif details the relationship between the four primary qualities and the four natural powers of the vegetative soul. It explains that while heat universally aids all powers, cold, dryness, and moisture have more limited roles. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping the Islamic philosophical account of biological processes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the technical terms for the four powers and qualities. Pay attention to the commentary phrases.
The passage includes both the original text and al-Jurjani's explanatory remarks.
2
Identify which qualities serve which powers
From the passage, list for each quality (heat, cold, dryness, moisture) which of the four powers it serves. Note any indirect service.
The summary at the end gives a clear breakdown.
3
Understand the reasoning for cold's indirect service
The passage explains that cold, though inherently opposed to the powers, assists the retentive and expulsive powers indirectly. Study how it does so.
Cold helps hold fibers and thickens wind, aiding retention and expulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثالث : القسم الأول : النفس النباتية
استرخاء بسبب الرطوبة لتعسر الحركة. وحيث كانت الحركة في الجاذبة أقوى كانت حاجتها إلى اليبوسة أشد ) والهاضمة لا حاجة لها إلى اليبس بل إلى الرطوبة ) المعينة إياه في التفريق والجمع والطبخ والإنضاج والبرودة مع كونها منافية بالذات لأفعال هذه تخدم بالعرض الماسكة بإعانتها على حبس الليف المورب على هيئة الاشتمال الصالح للإمساك، وتخدم كذلك الدافعة بأنها تمنع تحليل الريح المعينة على الدفع وأيضا تغلظها. وكلما كانت الريح أغلظ كانت أعون وأيضا تجمع الليف العاصر وتكثفه فتكون أقوى في الدفع فظهر ما ذكر أن الحرارة تخدم جميع هذه القوى، والبرودة لا تخدم إلا الماسكة والدافعة وأن اليبوسة تخدم ما سوى الهاضمة، والرطوبة تخدمها فقط.

قوله : ( وحيث كانت الحركة في الجاذبة (أقوى هذا إشارة إلى وجه الترتيب بين والدافعة كما دل عليه بكلمة ثم وحاصله أن حاجة كل منهما إلى اليبوسة إنما هو لأجل التسكين الممكن للتحيرك فما كان تحريكه أقوى كان حاجته إلى اليبوسة أكثر. وقوله : وتخدم أي تخدم البرودة. وقوله : كذلك أي بالعرض وقوله تغلظها من التغليظ أي تغلظ البرودة الريح : المنحنية وهذا من الإعانة .
The first observatory - the third chapter - the first section: the plant soul. Relaxation due to moisture because of the difficulty of movement. And since movement in the attractive [power] is stronger, its need for dryness is more intense. And the digestive [power] has no need for dryness but rather for moisture, which assists it in differentiation, combination, cooking, and maturation. And cold, despite being inherently opposed to the actions of these [powers], serves indirectly the retentive [power] by helping it to hold the oblique fibers in the form of containment suitable for retention, and likewise serves the expulsive [power] by preventing the dissolution of the wind that aids expulsion and also thickens it. And whenever the wind is thicker, it is more helpful, and also it collects the constrictive fibers and condenses them, so it becomes stronger in expulsion. So what was mentioned appears: that heat serves all these powers, and cold serves only the retentive and expulsive, and dryness serves what is other than the digestive, and moisture serves only it. His statement: 'And since movement in the attractive is stronger' – this is an indication of the aspect of ordering between [the attractive] and the expulsive, as indicated by the word 'then'. Its summary is that the need of each of them for dryness is only for the sake of possible stillness for movement, so whatever has stronger movement has a greater need for dryness. His statement: 'and it serves' – meaning cold serves. His statement: 'likewise' – meaning indirectly. His statement: 'thickens it' – from thickening, meaning cold thickens the wind: it bends it, and this is assistance.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the role of the four qualities (heat, cold, dryness, moisture) in relation to the four natural powers (attractive, retentive, digestive, expulsive) of the plant soul. It begins by noting that relaxation occurs due to moisture because moisture makes movement difficult. Since the attractive power has stronger movement, it requires more dryness to stabilize it, while the digestive power needs moisture to aid in breaking down and combining food. The commentary then explains that cold, although intrinsically opposed to the actions of these powers, indirectly assists the retentive power by helping to hold the oblique fibers in place, and also assists the expulsive power by preventing the dissolution of gases and thickening them, making them more effective for expulsion. The conclusion summarizes: heat helps all four powers, cold only helps the retentive and expulsive, dryness helps all except the digestive, and moisture helps only the digestive. The subsequent commentary clarifies the author's phrasing: 'and since movement in the attractive is stronger' points to the ordering between the attractive and expulsive powers; the need for dryness is to make movement possible while providing stability. The phrase 'it serves' refers to cold serving, 'likewise' means indirectly, and 'thickens it' means cold thickens the wind, which is a form of assistance.

Will you ask Allah to show you the hidden help in what feels like opposition?
?
The digestive power requires moisture to break down and transform food. In your own spiritual 'digestion'—the process of taking in knowledge and making it part of you—where are you relying on dryness (rigid routines, harsh self-judgment) when you actually need the moisture of patience, gentleness, and flexibility? What specific step can you take today to bring more spiritual moisture to that area?
