Vision Through Transparent Media: Straight and Bent Paths
Iji discusses how vision occurs through rectilinear and bent paths in different media on both theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Iji explains the effect of different transparent media on vision, both on the emission and impression theories, and why the bending angle cannot equal the visual angle.

Core Idea
The bending of light explains why objects look larger in water.

This passage is a note (tanbih) that addresses a subtle point in optics: how vision occurs through transparent media of varying density. Iji explains the phenomenon on both the emission and impression theories, focusing on the change in perceived size. He also corrects a common misconception about the angle of refraction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Get an overview of the whole note. Notice that it has two main parts: an explanation of how rays travel through different media, and a correction of a mistaken assumption about the bending angle.
2
Trace the emission theory explanation
Follow how the radial lines form a cone from the eye. In a uniform medium like air, they go straight. In a dissimilar medium like water, they bend toward the axis, making the object appear larger. If the disparity is reversed, they bend away, making it appear smaller.
This part explains the mechanics of the widely accepted theory.
3
Contrast with the impression theory
According to the impression theory, there is no actual cone or straight lines—only imagined ones. So the change in perceived size is due to variation in the cone's vertex angle and the part of the crystalline lens involved.
Iji presents this as an alternative but less concrete explanation.
4
Examine the correction about the angle of refraction
Some mistakenly think the angle of bending equals the angle of vision. The passage shows this is impossible: if it were equal, the object would appear enormously enlarged when bending toward the axis, or not at all when bending away.
The detailed proof is deferred, but the absurdity is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : سواء قلنا الإبصار بالانطباع أو بخروج الشعاع فإنه ينفذ في الجسم الشفاف المتوسط فيما بين الرائي والمرئي كالهواء مستقيما وينفذ في الشفاف الذي شفيفه مخالف لشفيف الهواء كالماء والبخار منعطفاً) هذا إنما يظهر على القول بخروج الشعاع فإن الخطوط الشعاعية التي على سطح المخروط كما مرت إليه إشارة في صدر الكتاب تنفذ إلى المرئي على الاستقامة إلى طرفيه إذا كان الشفاف المتوسط متشابه الغلظ والرقة فإن فرض هناك تفاوت بأن يكون ما يلي الرائي هواء وما يلي المرئي ماء مثلا فإن تلك الخطوط إذا وصلت إلى ذلك الماء انعطفت ومالت إلى سهم المخروط ثم وصلت إلى طرفي المرئي فتكون زاوية رأس المخروط هاهنا أكبر منها في الصورة الأولى فلذلك يرى المرئي أعظم ولو انعكس الفرض مالت الخطوط إلى خلاف جانب السهم فترى أصغر . وأما على القول بالانطباع فليس هناك مخروط ولا خطوط مستقيمة نافذة في الشفاف على الاستقامة أو الانعطاف إلا على سبيل التوهم المحض والتخيل الصرف فيختلف حال زاوية رأس المخروط والجزء الواقع فيها من الجليدية فيتفاوت أيضاً المرئي الواحد صغراً وكبراً، ثم إن الانعطاف إلى جهة السهم أو خلافها إنما يكون ) بزاوية أصغر من زاوية الرؤية بكثير ومن تصور أنها مثل زاوية الرؤية فقد أخطأ ، وموضع بيانه غير هذا الموضع) وقد بينه بعض من عاصره المصنف من محققي صناعة المناظر أنه ينعكس الشعاع البصري
قوله: (مستقيماً) وكذا قوله منعطفاً حال من الضمير المستتر في ينفذ وهذا الضمير المستتر راجع إلى الإبصار المذكور نظراً إلى اعتبار الشعاع فيه محققاً أو موهوماً على ما أشار إليه الشارح وقوله هناك أي في صورة التفاوت المذكور منها أي من زاوية رأس المخروط حال كونها في الصورة الأولى أي في صورة تشابه الغلظ والرقة .
قوله : ( فقد أخطأ فإن زاوية الانعطاف كانت مساوية لزاوية الرؤية فإذا فرضنا أن يكون الشعاع النافذ بما على سطح المرئي مثلاً . لزم أن يرى ذلك المرئي أضعاف أضعاف مقداره في صورة الانعطاف إلى جهة السهم ولزم أن لا يرى ذلك المرئي أصلا في صورة الانعطاف إلى خلاف جهة السهم وذلك لأن زاوية الرؤية على الفرض المذكور آنفاً تكون هي الزاوية القائمة
A note: Whether we say vision is by impression or by emission of a ray, it penetrates the transparent body mediating between the seer and the seen, like air, in a straight line, and it penetrates a transparent body whose transparency differs from that of air, like water and vapor, in a bent path. This appears only on the saying of the emission of a ray, for the radial lines that are on the surface of the cone, as has been pointed out earlier in the book, penetrate to the seen object in a straight line to its two sides if the intermediate transparent body is uniform in thickness. But if there is a disparity, such that what is next to the seer is air and what is next to the seen is water, for example, then those lines when they reach that water bend and incline towards the axis of the cone, then reach the two sides of the seen object. Thus the angle at the vertex of the cone here is larger than in the first case, and therefore the seen object is seen as larger. If the situation were reversed, the lines would incline to the opposite side of the axis, and it is seen as smaller. As for the saying by impression, there is no cone nor straight lines penetrating the transparent in straightness or bending, except by pure supposition and sheer imagination. So the state of the angle at the vertex of the cone and the part of the crystalline lens that lies within it varies, so the same object also varies in smallness and bigness. Then the bending towards the axis or its opposite occurs with an angle much smaller than the angle of vision, and whoever imagines it equals the angle of vision has erred. And its explanation is not this place. And some of the contemporaries of the author among the verifiers of the science of optics have explained that the visual ray is reflected. His saying: (straight) and likewise his saying: bent are adverbs of state from the implied pronoun in 'penetrates,' and this implied pronoun refers to the aforementioned vision, considering the ray in it as actual or imaginary, as the commentator indicated. And his saying: 'there' meaning in the case of the mentioned disparity, 'from it' meaning from the angle at the vertex of the cone, while it is in the first case i.e. in the case of uniform thickness. His saying: (he has erred) for the angle of bending equaled the angle of vision. So if we suppose that the penetrating ray is upon the surface of the seen object, for example, then that seen object would be seen as many times its size in the case of bending towards the axis, and would not be seen at all in the case of bending opposite the axis, because the angle of vision in the aforementioned supposition is a right angle.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbih) on how vision operates through transparent media. It states that on both theories (emission and impression), vision penetrates transparent bodies. In a uniform transparent medium (like air), it goes straight; in a medium of different transparency (like water or vapor), it bends. The explanation is primarily given on the emission theory: the radial lines forming a cone from the eye travel straight to the object when the medium is uniform. When the medium near the object differs (e.g., air to water), the lines bend towards the axis of the cone, making the object appear larger. If the disparity is reversed, the lines bend away from the axis, making the object appear smaller. On the impression theory, there is no actual cone, only an imagined one, so the variation in perceived size is accounted for differently. The passage then addresses the angle of bending: it is much smaller than the angle of vision. Some mistakenly think the bending angle equals the visual angle; this error is corrected by a thought experiment: if the bending angle were equal, the object would either be seen as enormously enlarged or not at all, which is false. The detailed explanation is deferred to another place.

الجليدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The angle of refraction (bending) is equal to the angle of vision.
Correction: The passage shows that the bending angle is much smaller. If it were equal, the object would appear either enormously enlarged or not visible at all.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when the visual ray passes from air to water, what happens to the lines of the cone?
A) They continue straight.
B) They bend away from the axis.
C) They bend towards the axis, making the object appear larger.
D) They disappear.

Key Takeaway
The bending of visual rays in different transparent media alters the perceived size of objects, and the angle of bending is much smaller than the angle of vision, contrary to a common mistake.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look back on a past decision that turned out poorly, can you identify a 'bent' perspective—something that made a problem seem larger or a benefit smaller than it was? What was the 'medium' (pride, fear, haste) that caused that distortion? How can you ensure your spiritual vision is through a straight medium next time?
?
This passage shows that even our physical sight can be systematically deceived by the medium. In your faith, what 'media' might distort how you see your Lord's signs or your own soul? Are you willing to examine and adjust your vision so that you see things as they truly are, no matter how uncomfortable?

Definition of Reflection and the Equality of Angles
Al-Iji defines optical reflection and states the law of equal angles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometric condition for optical reflection as defined by Al-Iji, specifically the equality of angles between the incident and reflected rays.

Core Idea
Reflection obeys a simple law: the angle of incidence equals the angle of reflection.

Al-Iji begins by describing the phenomenon of optical reflection from polished surfaces. He sets up a geometric diagram with a pupil, water surface, and object, then states the fundamental law: the angle of the incident ray equals the angle of the reflected ray. This passage forms the basis for understanding how light behaves when it bounces off surfaces.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Familiarise yourself with the terms and the geometric setup described.
2
Identify the key elements
Look for the polished surface, the incident ray, the reflected ray, and the angles mentioned. Al-Iji uses specific letters (W, H, B, A, J, K) to label points.
3
Understand the law of reflection
The passage states that angle A B J (angle of incidence) equals angle H B K (angle of reflection). This is the core law of reflection.
4
Consider the special case
When the incident ray is perpendicular to the surface, the reflected ray coincides with it, making the angle between them zero. This is an important special case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغيره من السطح الصقيل كالمرآة والماء إلى ما يقابله بزاوية مساوية لزاوية الرؤية يعني زاوية الشعاع وليكن لتصوير انعكاس الحدقة وح ك سطح الماء و حب هو المرئي من سطحه و هـ مقابل المرئي بحيث يكون وضعه منه كوضعه من الحدقة، ف الخط الشعاعي النافذ إلى المرئي ، و هـب الشعاع المنعكس، وزاوية ا ب ج زاوية الشعاع على سطح المرئي من جانب ح وزاوية هـ ب كزاوية الانعكاس عليه وهي مساوية للزاوية الأولى ولما تساويا وجب أن يتساوى أيضاً زاويتا آ ب كـ هـ ب ج ، وأما زاوية ا ب هـ فهي الواقعة بين خطي الشعاع النافذ والمنعكس، وقد تنتفي هذه الزاوية كما إذا كان الخط النافذ قائما على سطح المرئي فينطبق عليه الخط المنعكس.
And other than it from the polished surface such as the mirror and water to what faces it with an angle equal to the angle of vision, meaning the angle of the ray. And let it be for depicting the reflection of the pupil: W H is the surface of the water, and H B is the visible object from its surface, and H is opposite the visible object such that its position relative to it is like its position relative to the pupil. So the ray line penetrating to the visible object, and H B is the reflected ray. And angle A B J is the angle of the ray on the surface of the visible object from side H, and angle H B K is the angle of reflection upon it, and it is equal to the first angle. And when they are equal, it is necessary that also angles A B K and H B J be equal. And as for angle A B H, it is the angle lying between the lines of the penetrating ray and the reflected ray, and this angle may disappear, as when the penetrating ray is perpendicular to the surface of the visible object, so the reflected ray coincides with it.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage defines optical reflection and its essential geometric condition. The author begins by describing a polished surface like a mirror or water. He sets up a diagram: point H is the pupil, line W H is the water surface, H B is the object, and H is opposite the object. The incident ray from the pupil to the object is drawn, and the reflected ray from the object to the pupil is H B. The angle A B J is the angle of incidence on the object's surface from side H, and angle H B K is the angle of reflection, equal to the first. From this equality, it follows that angles A B K and H B J are also equal. The angle A B H is the angle between the incident and reflected rays; it may be zero when the incident ray is perpendicular, causing the reflected ray to coincide with it. This is a standard geometric statement of the law of reflection.

Key Terms
انعكاس
زاوية الشعاع
زاوية الانعكاس
الشعاع النافذ
الشعاع المنعكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the law of reflection involves equal distances or that the incident and reflected rays are symmetric in length.
Correction: The law only states equality of angles, not distances. The passage correctly focuses on angles, not distances.

Checkpoint
What condition must be true for the angle between the incident and reflected rays to disappear?
A) The incident ray is at 45 degrees.
B) The incident ray is perpendicular to the surface.
C) The reflected ray is parallel to the surface.
D) The surface is curved.

Key Takeaway
The law of reflection states that the angle of incidence equals the angle of reflection, which is the fundamental principle for understanding how light reflects off polished surfaces.

Refraction and the Inversion of Images in Water
Al-Iji defines refraction and explains why objects appear inverted in water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Iji's geometric definition of refraction and why objects appear inverted in water due to the bending of light rays.

Core Idea
Refraction bends light, fooling the mind into seeing an inverted image.

This passage from al-Iji's work presents a sophisticated optical analysis within a kalam context. He defines refraction using a geometric model and then explains a puzzling everyday phenomenon: why a tree on the riverbank appears inverted in the water. The explanation hinges on the law of reflection and the mind's habitual reliance on straight-line vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic excerpt slowly, then its literal translation. Let the technical terms settle in your mind before analyzing the argument.
Key terms: انعطاف (refraction), زاوية الانعطاف (angle of refraction), خطان أحمران/أسودان (red/black lines).
2
Understand the geometric setup
Identify the elements: the pupil (H), the visible object (AB), the axis of the cone, and the two sets of lines (straight red vs. bent black). Notice how the density of the medium changes what the lines represent.
The 'red lines' represent straight rays in a uniform medium; the 'black lines' are the refracted rays in a non-uniform medium.
3
Grasp the inversion explanation
Follow the 'subtle indication' where al-Iji constructs a diagram of a river, a tree, and the pupil. He shows that rays reflecting off water to different heights on the tree create longer paths to the top, making the mind place the top deeper underground, thus inverting the image.
The soul is unaware of the reflection due to habit, so it interprets the longer ray as indicating greater depth.
4
Reflect on the kalam context
Consider why a theologian would discuss optics. Al-Iji is demonstrating how ordinary sensory experiences can be explained by consistent physical laws, supporting a worldview where natural phenomena are governed by rational principles (which ultimately depend on God).
This passage also shows the use of 'luzum' (necessary consequences) common in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

على زاوية مساوية لزاوية الشعاع (لوازم) كثيرة ( من رؤية الشجر على الشط منتكساً (و) رؤية (العنبة في الماء هي فرضنا وقوع زاوية الانعطاف فلا بد أن تحدث في كل من جانبي السهم المخروط زاوية انعطاف، فعلى تقدير مساواة زاوية الانعطاف لزاوية الرؤية يلزم أن تحدث في جانبي السهم زاويتان هما القائمتان وظاهر أن ما بين ضلعي زاوية الانعطاف يكون مرئياً في صورة الانعطاف إلى جهة السهم ويكون غير مرئي في صورة الانعطاف إلى خلاف جهة السهم، وظاهر أيضاً أن القائمتين لو كانتا مجتمعتين في سطح الماء الذي تحته العنبة فرضاً لم تبق هناك زاوية أصلاً فيلزم أن يرى المرئي في الماء بقدر عرض الماء بالغاً ما بلغ في الصورة الزاويتين المذكورتين مرئياً أصلاً في الصورة الثانية فتأمل والله الموفق. قوله : (أنه ينعكس إلخ ) عطف على قوله : فإنه ينفذ إلخ . وقوله : بزاوية مساوية لزاوية الرؤية وهي زاوية الانعكاس فإنها مساوية متساويتين بدون اعتبار القدر المشترك يلزم أن تكونا متساويتين أيضاً مع اعتبار انضمام القدر المشترك إلى كل واحدة منهما . شطه كالإجاصة ونحوهما لسنا الآن بصدد بيانها فإنه خروج عن الصناعة الكلامية بالكلية . أما رؤية العنبة كذلك فمن لوازم الانعطاف لأن زاوية الخطين الأسودين عند الحدقة أكبر من زاوية الأحمرين كما مر ذلك في المرصد الرابع من الموقف الأول، وأما رؤية الشجر منتكساً فمن لوازم تساوي زاويتي الشعاع كانعكاسية ط و ب وهذه الانعكاسية أصغر من زاوية ح و ب الخارجة عن مثلث رك و فشعاعية هـ و ا أصغر أيضاً من هذه الخارجة. ثم نقول زاوية هـ ك ا أكبر للعلة المذكورة من زاوية هـ و ا المساوية لزاوية ح ب و فتكون أكبر منها أيضاً فيلزم أن يكون كل من زاويتي هـ كـاح و ب أكبر من الأخرى هذا خلف. وإما أنه لا يجوز أن ينعكس من نقطة واحد كـك مثلاً خطان إلى نقطتين من الشجر كنقطتي حاط فلاستلزامه مساواة الكل والجزء لشيء واحد كما لا يخفى
And as for depicting refraction: it is that you suppose H is the pupil and A B is the visible object. So if the transparent medium is of uniform consistency, then the lines reaching the two ends of the visible object are the two straight red lines. And if it is different such that what is closer to the viewer is denser, then the lines reaching them are the two black lines that are bent from straightness toward the axis of the cone. And the angle of refraction is the imagined angle from the bent line, assuming straightness and refraction, like angle H K. (And due to this) that we mentioned of refraction and reflection at an angle equal to the angle of the ray, there are many consequences (such as seeing the tree on the shore inverted, and seeing the grape in water. We assumed the occurrence of the angle of refraction, so it is necessary that on each side of the axis of the cone an angle of refraction occurs. On the assumption of equality of the angle of refraction to the angle of vision, it follows that on the two sides of the axis two right angles occur. And it is apparent that what is between the two sides of the angle of refraction is visible in the case of refraction toward the axis, and is not visible in the case of refraction away from the axis. And it is also apparent that if the two right angles were combined on the water surface under which is the grape, hypothetically, no angle would remain at all; so it would follow that the visible object in water is seen in the measure of the width of the water, reaching whatever it reaches in the first image, so that visible object would be seen many times its size—this is a contradiction. And that it would not be seen at all in the second image, since it is assumed that if it increased in refraction, the two were right angles, and there was nothing between the two sides of each of the mentioned angles visible at all in the second image. So reflect, and God is the Successor. His saying: 'that it reflects, etc.' is conjoined to his saying: 'for it penetrates, etc.' And his saying: 'at an angle equal to the angle of vision'—it is the angle of reflection, for it is equal to the angle of vision which is the angle of the ray, as he explained. His saying: 'it is necessary that also the two angles B D and H B J be equal'—that is because angle A B H is a common measure between angle A B D and angle H B J. So since the two mentioned angles are equal without considering the common measure, it is necessary that they be equal also with consideration of adding the common measure to each of them. As for seeing the grape and so on, we are not now to explain them, for it is a departure from the science of kalam entirely. As for seeing the grape likewise, it is a consequence of refraction, because the angle of the two black lines at the pupil is greater than the angle of the two red lines, as passed in the fourth observatory of the first stand. And as for seeing the tree inverted, it is a consequence of the equality of the two angles of the ray and reflection. We will hint at it here with a subtle indication: that we suppose line A B is the width of the river, and line H B is the tree standing upright, and H is the pupil. And we suppose on A B two points K and W, and on H B two points H and T. So if from H a ray line goes out to W, and another to K, it is necessary that the first reflect to point T, for example, so the ray angle, namely angle H W A, becomes like the reflection angle, namely angle T W B. And the other reflects to point H, so also the ray angle H W A and the reflection angle H K B are equal, so that the reflected lines from the water surface to the tree become like the strings of the convex instrument called 'jang' as passed in that observatory. So the reflection to the top of the tree is longer than the reflection to what is beneath it. And the soul is not aware of the reflection, due to its habit of seeing by the rays going out straight, so the top of the tree is, for it, deeper in the water, and so on down to its bottom, so you see it inverted, its head farther from the water surface, very much sunk in it. And it is not permissible that the line from K to T reflect, and from W to H and elsewhere, unless its ray angle H W A is like the reflection angle T W B, and this reflection angle is smaller than angle H W B external to triangle R K W, so the ray angle H W A is also smaller than this external angle. Then we say: angle H K A is greater, for the mentioned reason, than angle H W A which is equal to angle H B W, so it is also greater than it. Then it follows that both angles H K H and W B are each greater than the other—this is a contradiction. And if it is not permissible that from one point, e.g. K, two lines reflect to two points of the tree, like points H and T, because it would require equality of the whole and the part to one thing, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage defines refraction (in'itaf) and explains its consequences for vision, particularly why objects appear inverted in water. The author begins by setting up a geometric model: the pupil at H, the object AB, and a transparent medium. When the medium is uniform, light travels straight (red lines). When it varies in density (denser near the viewer), light bends (black lines) toward the axis of the cone, creating an angle of refraction. This leads to phenomena like seeing a tree on the shore inverted and a grape enlarged in water. The author then presents a subtle explanation for the inversion: he constructs a diagram of a river (AB) with a tree (HB) and the pupil (H). Light rays from the pupil reflect off the water surface to different points on the tree. The reflected rays are like the strings of a convex harp, with the ray to the top of the tree being longer, making the top appear deeper. The mind, accustomed to straight-line vision, interprets this as the tree being inverted. The author then addresses an objection: certain combinations of reflections would lead to contradictions (e.g., a ray reflecting to two points), showing that the reflection must follow the law of equal angles. This detailed geometric argument demonstrates the logical consistency of the refraction explanation within the framework of optics.

Key Terms
انعطاف
زاوية الانعطاف
سهم المخروط
الخطان الأحمران
الخطان الأسودان
انعكاس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The inversion of images in water is due to a 180-degree flip of the image.
Correction: Al-Iji shows that inversion results from the mind misinterpreting longer reflected rays as greater depth, not from a straightforward reversal.

Checkpoint
According to al-Iji, why does the soul see the tree inverted in water?
A) Because the water surface acts as a mirror and flips the image.
B) Because the reflected ray to the top of the tree is longer, making the mind place the top deeper.
C) Because the grape in water appears enlarged, confusing the observer.
D) Because the angle of incidence is greater than the angle of reflection.

Key Takeaway
Refraction bends light toward the axis of the cone, and the mind's habit of straight-line vision creates the illusion of inversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The soul was not aware of the reflection due to its habit of seeing by straight rays. What habits in your own thinking might be causing you to misinterpret what you see in your life or in the world around you? Will you commit to actively seeking the deeper, often counterintuitive truths that God has placed in His creation?
?
Al-Iji used precise geometric reasoning to uncover the true cause of the inverted image. How often do you settle for surface-level understanding of your faith instead of engaging in deep study? What step can you take this week to replace a lazy assumption about Allah or His attributes with a studied certainty?
?
The law of reflection is consistent: the angle of incidence always equals the angle of reflection. Yet this very consistency, combined with the mind's habit, led to illusion. Does your reliance on the regularity of life's patterns sometimes blind you to Allah's direct action and power? How can you remind yourself daily that the All-Powerful is not bound by the habits He has created?

Common Sensibles and Accidental Perception
Defines common sensibles and explains their accidental perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why common sensibles do not require separate sense faculties because they are perceived accidentally through the proper sensibles.

Core Idea
Common sensibles are perceived accidentally, not essentially, so no extra faculties are needed.

This passage explores the concept of common sensibles—things like magnitude, number, and motion—that are perceived by more than one external sense. The author addresses a potential objection: if each type of sensible required its own sense faculty, we would need many more faculties. The solution is that these are sensed accidentally, through qualities like color or heat, by the same faculties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common sensibles
Read the passage and list the common sensibles mentioned: magnitudes, numbers, positions, shapes, motion, rest, proximity, distance, and contact.
These are examples; the category is broader.
2
Understand the objection and answer
Notice the objection that each type of sensible would require its own faculty. The answer is that common sensibles are sensed accidentally (bil-‘araḍ), not essentially.
Accidental perception means they are perceived through the proper sensibles like color or heat.
3
Follow the detailed explanation per sense
Read how vision, touch, taste, smell, and hearing perceive common sensibles through intermediaries. Note that hearing infers magnitude from loudness.
The passage shows that no new faculties are needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : هاهنا محسوسات مشتركة أي يشترك في إدراكها الحواس الظاهرة فلا يحتاج في الإحساس بها إلى قوى أخرى كالمقادير والأعداد والأوضاع والأشكال والحركة والسكون والقرب والبعد والمماسة فلو وجب لكل نوع محسوس قوة على حده كما ذهب إليه جمع لوجب إثبات قوى أخرى لإدراك هذه الأمور لأنها أنواع متخالفة وقد يجاب عنها بأنها محسوسة بالعرض لا بالذات أي بالتبعية لا بالأصالة فلا حاجة فيها إلى قوة أخرى كما أشرنا إليه إنما ذاك فيما هو محسوس بالذات وقد بين كونها محسوسة بالعرض بقوله: وأنها إنما تحس بواسطة اللون والضوء والحرارة والبرودة ونحوها وتفصيله أن يقال : إن البصر يحس بالعظم والعدد والوضع والشكل والحركة والسكون والمماسة يتوسط الضوء واللون واللمس يدرك جميعها بتوسط حر أو برد وصلابة أو لين والذوق يدرك العظم بأن يذوق طعماً كثيراً، والعدد بأن يجد طعوماً مختلفة، والشم يدرك العدد بضرب من القياس وهو أن يعلم أن الذي انقطعت رائحته غير الذي حصلت رائحته ثانياً، ويدرك الحركة والسكون بواسطة اللمس إدراكا ضعيفاً وأما السمع فإنه لا يدرك العظم ولكنه قد يدل عليه أحياناً من جهة أن الأصوات العظيمة إنما تحصل في الأغلب من أجسام عظيمة
And the second [type]: here are common sensibles, i.e., the external senses share in perceiving them, so no other faculties are needed to sense them, such as magnitudes, numbers, positions, shapes, motion, rest, proximity, distance, and contact. Thus if a separate power were necessary for each type of sensible, as a group held, it would be necessary to posit other powers to perceive these things because they are different types. And it is answered about them that they are sensed accidentally, not essentially, i.e., by way of subordination, not originally, so there is no need for another power, as we indicated—that is only for what is sensed essentially. And he explained that they are sensed accidentally by his saying: And they are only sensed through color, light, heat, cold, and the like. And its detail is that one says: vision senses magnitude, number, position, shape, motion, rest, and contact through light and color; touch perceives all of them through hot or cold, hardness or softness; taste perceives magnitude by tasting a large amount, and number by finding different tastes; smell perceives number by a kind of inference, which is that it knows that the one whose scent ceased is not the one whose scent arose secondly, and it perceives motion and rest through touch weakly; as for hearing, it does not perceive magnitude but may sometimes indicate it because loud sounds mostly come from large bodies.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage defines the common sensibles (al-mahsūsāt al-mushtaraka) and addresses the objection that they would require separate faculties. The author begins by listing the common sensibles: magnitudes, numbers, positions, shapes, motion, rest, proximity, distance, and contact. These are perceived by more than one external sense, so if each type required its own faculty, many unnecessary faculties would have to be assumed. The answer is that these are perceived accidentally (bil-‘araḍ), not essentially (bil-dhāt), meaning they are sensed through the proper sensibles (e.g., color, light, heat) rather than directly. The author then details how each sense perceives these: vision through light and color; touch through temperature and texture; taste through quantity and variety of flavors; smell through inference; hearing through inference from loudness. This shows that no additional powers are needed; the common sensibles are known by the same faculties that perceive the proper sensibles.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because common sensibles are perceived by multiple senses, each sense must have a separate faculty for each common sensible.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they are perceived accidentally, not essentially, so no new faculties are needed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do common sensibles not require separate faculties?
A) Because they are not real sensibles.
B) Because they are sensed accidentally through the proper sensibles.

D) Because each sense has its own faculty for them.

Key Takeaway
Common sensibles are perceived accidentally through the proper sensibles, so no additional faculties are required.

How does this apply to how you listen to the Quran—do you listen for the 'loud sounds' (the obvious meanings) or do you go deeper to perceive the magnitudes (the profound truths)? What will you change in your listening habits starting today?

Two Meanings of 'Sensed Accidentally' and Their Distinction
Distinguishes two meanings of 'sensed accidentally' and justifies the first as more appropriate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct meanings of 'sensed accidentally' and why the author considers the first meaning (perception through a medium) more appropriate for common sensibles.

Core Idea
There are two senses of 'sensed accidentally': indirect perception and mere accompaniment; only the first is truly sensory.

This passage from al-Mawaqif tackles a subtle conceptual distinction. The term 'sensed accidentally' can refer to two different things: something perceived indirectly through another sensed quality, or something not sensed at all but simply associated with a sensed object. The author argues that only the first meaning is proper, and this distinction is crucial for understanding how common sensibles (like magnitude) are perceived. The following text explains this distinction using the example of whiteness and color, and contrasts it with recognizing someone's identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the author's use of 'قد' to indicate the second meaning is less appropriate.
2
Identify the two meanings
Locate where the author presents the two meanings: (1) sensed through a medium (like magnitude through color), (2) not sensed at all but concomitant (like seeing someone as Abu Amr).
The author says these are homonyms (ishtirāk lafẓī).
3
Understand the analogy of whiteness
The author uses the example of whiteness subsisting in the surface essentially and in the body accidentally, to illustrate how one thing can be attributed in two ways.
This parallels color being visible essentially and magnitude accidentally.
4
Grasp the conclusion and refutation
The author concludes that common sensibles are sensed subordinately, and the first meaning of 'sensed accidentally' is more appropriate. The objection from al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya is refuted by showing equivocation.
The fair-minded person can distinguish between sensing magnitude and recognizing paternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد مر استعانة الشم والسمع بالعقل في العدد والعظم ثم أشار إلى معنى آخر للمحسوس بالعرض بقوله : وقد يقال : المحسوس بالعرض ما لا يحس به أصلا لكن يقارن المحسوس بالحقيقة كإبصارنا أبا عمرو، فإن المحسوس ذلك الشخص وليس كونه أبا عمرو محسوساً أصلا لا أصالة ولا تبعاً بخلاف الأمور السابقة فإنها محسوسة بالتبعية فإطلاق المحسوس بالعرض على هذين المعنيين بالاشتراك اللفظي وبهذا خرج الجواب عما ذكره في المباحث المشرقية من أن هذه الأمور ليست محسوسة بالعرض لأن المحسوس بالعرض ما لا يحس به حقيقة، لكنه مقارن للمحسوس الحقيقي، وإن شئت حقيقة الحال فاستمع لهذا المقال، الست قد سمعت أن البياض مثلاً قائم بالسطح أولاً وبالذات وقائم بالجسم ثانياً وبالعرض، ولا شبهة في أنه ليس معنى ذلك أن للبياض قيامين أحدهما بالسطح والآخر بالجسم، بل معناه أن له قياما واحداً بالسطح، لكن لما قام السطح بالجسم صار ذلك القيام منسوباً إلى السطح أولاً وبالذات، وإلى الجسم ثانياً وبالعرض فقس على ذلك معنى كون الشيء مثلاً مرئياً بالذات والعرض. فإذا قلنا : اللون مرئي بالذات كان معناه أن الرؤية متعلقة به بلا توسط تعلق تلك الرؤية بغيره وذلك لا ينافي كون رؤيته مشروطة برؤية أخرى متعلقة بالضوء فيكون كلاً منها مرئيين بالذات، لكن رؤية أحدهما مشروطة برؤية الآخر. وإذا قلنا : المقدار مرئي بالعرض بواسطة اللون كان معناه أن هناك رؤية واحدة متعلقة باللون أولاً وبالذات والمقدار ثانياً وبالعرض وهكذا الحال في سائر الأمور التي سماها مشتركة بين الحواس فهي محسوسة تبعاً قطعاً، وأما كون الشخص أبا عمرو فلا تعلق للإحساس به البتة والمنصف إذا رجع إلى نفسه وجد تفرقة ضرورية بينهما، وعلم أن المقدار مثلاً له انكشاف في الحس ليس ذلك الانكشاف للأبوة فاتضح الفرق بين معنى المحسوس بالعرض، واندفع ما ذكره الإمام بل نقول : إطلاق هذا الاسم على المعنى الأول أولى كما أشار إليه المصنف بإيراد كلمة قد في المعنى الآخر
And the use of smell and hearing of reason in number and magnitude has passed. Then he pointed to another meaning of 'sensed accidentally' by his saying: And it may be said: the sensed accidentally is that which is not sensed at all but is concomitant with the real sensible, like our seeing Abu Amr, for the sensed is that person, and his being Abu Amr is not sensed at all, neither originally nor subordinately, unlike the previous matters, for they are sensed subordinately. So the application of 'sensed accidentally' to these two meanings is by homonymy. And by this the answer emerges against what he mentioned in al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya, that these matters are not sensed accidentally because the sensed accidentally is what is not truly sensed but is concomitant with the real sensible. And if you wish the truth of the matter, listen to this discourse: Have you not heard that whiteness, for example, subsists in the surface primarily and essentially, and subsists in the body secondarily and accidentally, and there is no doubt that the meaning is not that whiteness has two subsistences, one in the surface and the other in the body, but rather it has one subsistence in the surface, but when the surface subsists in the body, that subsistence becomes attributed to the surface primarily and essentially, and to the body secondarily and accidentally. So analogize to that the meaning of a thing being visible essentially and accidentally. For if we say: color is visible essentially, it means that vision is attached to it without the mediation of that vision's attachment to something else, and that does not contradict its vision being conditioned by another vision attached to light, so each of them is visible essentially, but the vision of one is conditioned by the vision of the other. And if we say: magnitude is visible accidentally through color, it means that there is one vision attached to color first and essentially, and to magnitude secondarily and accidentally. And such is the case in all the matters he called common among the senses: they are sensed subordinately without doubt. As for a person being Abu Amr, sensation has no connection to it at all. And the fair-minded person, if he returns to himself, finds an essential distinction between them, and knows that magnitude, for example, has a disclosure in sensation that is not the disclosure for paternity. So the difference between the two meanings of 'sensed accidentally' becomes clear, and what the Imam mentioned is refuted. Rather we say: applying this name to the first meaning is more appropriate, as the author indicated by using the word 'qad' (قد) for the other meaning.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage distinguishes two meanings of 'sensed accidentally' (al-mahsūs bil-‘araḍ). The first meaning is that something is perceived indirectly through another sensible (e.g., magnitude perceived through color). The second meaning is something that is not perceived at all but accompanies the perceived object (e.g., seeing someone as Abu Amr, where the person is seen but his identity is not sensed). The author argues that common sensibles fall under the first meaning because they are truly sensed, albeit through another quality. The objection from al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya is answered by showing that 'sensed accidentally' is used equivocally (ishtirāk lafẓī). The author illustrates with the analogy of whiteness subsisting in the surface essentially and in the body accidentally: one subsistence attributed differently. Similarly, color is visible essentially, but magnitude is visible accidentally through color. The fair-minded person can distinguish between sensing magnitude and recognizing someone as Abu Amr. Therefore, the first meaning (sensing through a medium) is the proper one, and the author's use of the word 'qad' for the second meaning indicates its lesser status.

محسوس بالحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that anything associated with a sensed object (like a person's identity) is sensed accidentally in the same way as magnitude through color.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only things perceived through a sensible (like magnitude) are truly sensed accidentally; mere intellectual accompaniment (like paternity) is not sensed at all.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which meaning of 'sensed accidentally' is more appropriate for common sensibles like magnitude?
A) The meaning where something is not sensed at all but accompanies the sensible.
B) The meaning where something is sensed through another sensible by way of subordination.
C) Both meanings equally.
D) Neither meaning, because common sensibles are sensed essentially.

Key Takeaway
The term 'sensed accidentally' primarily refers to perceiving something through another sense (like magnitude through color), not to mere intellectual accompaniment like recognizing someone's identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Where in your spiritual life might you be mistaking the secondary for the primary—focusing on outward forms while missing the inner reality? Resolve to realign your attention to what is essential.

Definition and First Proof of the Common Sense
Defines the common sense and gives its first proof with objections and answers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) is and the first rational proof for its existence, along with key objections and answers.

Core Idea
Judging between different sensed qualities requires a single internal faculty beyond the external senses.

This passage introduces the first of the internal perceptive faculties: the common sense. It is defined as the faculty where images from the five senses converge, allowing the soul to perceive them. The author then presents the first proof for its existence, arguing that without it we couldn't compare sensations across modalities. The passage also addresses objections that the intellect might suffice, clarifying the roles of corporeal and intellectual faculties.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of common sense
Read the opening lines where the common sense is defined as the faculty in which images of particular sensibles are imprinted, and note its role as an instrument for the soul.
The term 'spies' (al-jawāsīs) refers to the external senses that gather data.
2
Study the first proof
Identify the key claim: without a single faculty perceiving all sensibles, we cannot judge between them. Consider the example of judging that a tangible object is the same as a colored object.
The proof hinges on the need for both parties of a judgment to be present to the judge.
3
Analyze the first objection and reply
Read the objection that the intellect is the judge. Then see the author's reply: particulars are perceived only by corporeal faculties, not by the intellect.
4
Analyze the second objection and reply
Examine the objection that if the same faculty perceives both particulars and universals, then the intellect could be the judge. Note the two-part reply: either the perceiver is not the same (breaking the proof), or presence can occur through multiple instruments.
This objection challenges the assumption that the perceiver of both must be one.
5
Reflect on glosses and final incomplete objection
Read the glosses on 'perceptive' and 'assistive', and note the final objection about the absence of matter from the external senses, which is left incomplete.
The final objection suggests that the imprinting in external senses is not enough when the object is absent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - النوع الثاني : القوة المدركة الباطنة أي القوى التي يكمل بها الإدراك الباطني سواء كانت مدركة أو معينة في الإدراك ) وهي أيضاً خمس : الأولى : الحس المشترك وهي القوة التي ترتسم فيها صور الجزئيات المحسوسة بالحواس الخمس الظاهرة التي الجواسيس لها ( فتطالعها النفس من ثمة فتدركها ولما كانت هذه القوى آلة للنفس في إدراكها سميت مدركة لها ( ويثبتها ) أي يدل على ثبوت الحس المشترك ) ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : لولا أن فينا قوة ) واحدة ( مدركة للمحسوسات (كلها بحيث ترتسم فيها بأسرها (لما أمكننا الحكم ببعض المحسوسات على بعضها إيجاباً ولا سلباً مثل أن نحكم بأن هذا الملموس هو هذا الملون ) ، أو ليس هذا الملون ) فإن القاضي ) الحاكم بالنسبة ) لا بد أن يحضره الخصمان ( أي المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به حتى يمكنه ملاحظة النسبة بينهما وإيقاع أحد طرفيها وليس شيء من القوى الظاهرة كذلك فلا بد من قوة باطنة ) فإن The First Observatory - The Second Type: The Internal Perceptive Faculty. That is, the faculties by which internal perception is completed, whether they are perceptive or assistive in perception. They are also five: the first is the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), which is the faculty in which are imprinted images of the particular sensibles perceived by the five external senses, which are the spies for them. Then the soul inspects them from there and perceives them. Since these faculties are instruments for the soul in its perception, they are called perceptive for it. And it is proven—that is, the existence of the common sense is indicated—by three proofs. The first: if there were not in us a single faculty perceiving all sensibles such that they are imprinted in it entirely, we would not be able to judge some sensibles in relation to others, affirmatively or negatively, like judging that this tangible thing is this colored thing, or that it is not this colored thing. For the judge—the one who compares the relation—must have the two parties present before him, that is, the thing judged and the thing by which it is judged, so that he can observe the relation between them and posit one of its two ends. None of the external faculties are like this, so there must be an internal faculty. If it is said: the judge is the intellect, so there is no need for another faculty, we reply: we will show that particulars are perceived only by corporeal faculties, so the intellect does not perceive them and does not judge concerning them. Rather, there must be a corporeal faculty that perceives them all together and judges among them. And someone may say: what do you say about our judgment that Zayd is a human? If the perceiver of both is one, then the perceiver of the particular is the perceiver of the universal, namely the intellect, since corporeal faculties cannot perceive universals; then it is possible that the judge among particular sensibles is the intellect. Otherwise—that is, if the perceiver of both is not one—the basis of the proof is invalid, which is that the judge must have the two parties present. If it is said: the judge is the intellect as you indicated first, but the imprinting of images of sensibles in it is impossible, therefore there must be a corporeal faculty in which all their images are imprinted so that their presence before it can be conceived. The answer: presence before the intellect does not necessarily require them to be together in one faculty; rather, perhaps it suffices that they are imprinted in multiple instruments for the intellect, like the external senses. His statement: 'whether they are perceptive' means as some hold, and his statement: 'or assistive' means as others hold. And his statement: 'the spies'—it is said: I spied (jasastu) the news and tajassastu, i.e., I investigated them, from the spy (jāsūs). And it is narrated from al-Khalīl: al-jawās. His statement: 'like the external senses.' If it is said: the sufficiency of the imprinting of images of sensibles for presence before the intellect only applies when the matter is present to those external senses. But here it is assumed that the matter is absent from those senses; then it is not conceivable that such imprinting suffices for presence before
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) as the first of five internal perceptive faculties and gives the first proof for its existence. The author begins by identifying the second type of internal faculty: the internal perceptive faculty, which completes internal perception. He notes that these faculties are of two kinds: some are directly perceptive, others assist in perception. The first of the five is the common sense, in which images of particular sensibles from the external senses are imprinted; the soul then inspects these images and perceives them. Since these faculties serve as instruments for the soul, they are called 'perceptive' for it. The author then states that the existence of the common sense is proven by three proofs, the first of which is presented here. The proof argues that if we did not have a single faculty that perceives all sensibles together, we could not judge one sensible in relation to another—for example, judging that this tangible object is the same as this colored object. Such a judgment requires a judge who has both parties present. The external senses each perceive only one type of sensible (e.g., sight, touch) and cannot compare across modalities. Therefore, an internal faculty is necessary. The passage then addresses two objections. The first objection claims that the intellect itself is the judge, making another faculty unnecessary. The author replies that particulars are only perceived by corporeal faculties, not by the intellect, so a corporeal faculty is needed. The second objection argues that if the same faculty perceives particulars and universals, then the intellect (which perceives universals) could also perceive particulars and be the judge. The author first points out that if the two are not the same, the proof's premise fails. The objector then suggests that even if the intellect cannot have images imprinted in it, a corporeal faculty could serve as a repository for the intellect's use. The author answers that presence before the intellect does not require all images to be in one faculty; it suffices that they are in multiple instruments like the external senses. Finally, the passage includes glosses on the terms 'perceptive', 'assistive', and 'spies', and an additional objection about the condition of matter being present, which is left incomplete.

Key Terms
الحس المشترك
القوة المدركة الباطنة
الجزئيات المحسوسة
العقل
القوى الجسمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that judgment between sensed particulars is done solely by the intellect or by external senses without needing an internal faculty.
Correction: The passage argues that external senses each perceive only one modality, and the intellect does not perceive particulars at all, so a common internal faculty is necessary for cross-modal comparison.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why is an internal faculty necessary for judging between sensed particulars?
A) Because the intellect cannot perceive particulars, and the external senses each perceive only one type of sensible and cannot compare them.
B) Because the external senses are too weak to perceive all sensibles at once.
C) Because the soul cannot directly perceive images without a physical medium.
D) Because the common sense is the only faculty that can judge.

Key Takeaway
The common sense is an internal faculty that gathers sensory images from all external senses, enabling the soul to compare and judge between different sensibles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a ripe apple and feel its smoothness, do you ever pause to consider that a single 'you' is aware of both qualities? How does this awareness challenge your assumption that your senses are enough? What does this imply about the reality of your self beyond physical organs?
?
The proof argues that without a common sense, you couldn't judge that the apple you see is the one you touch. Has this ever failed you? Reflect on a moment when your senses seemed to contradict each other. How did your inner sense resolve it? What does that tell you about your need for a stable inner faculty?
?
If the intellect cannot directly perceive particulars, as argued, then your ability to reason about concrete things depends on this internal faculty. Do you treat your inner senses with care, or do you neglect their purification? How might spiritual discipline affect your perception of truth?

Second Proof of the Common Sense: The Falling Drop and Rotating Flame
Provides the second proof for the common sense using perceptual illusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perceptual phenomena of a falling drop appearing as a line and a rotating flame appearing as a circle serve as a proof for the existence of the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak), and how this argument addresses an objection based on induction.

Core Idea
We see lines and circles where there are none — that shows an internal sense at work.

This lesson presents the second empirical proof for the common sense, drawn from everyday visual illusions. By examining how we perceive continuous shapes from moving objects, the author argues that an internal faculty beyond the external senses must be involved. The passage also includes a potential objection and its sophisticated response, deepening our understanding of the nature of perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow of the argument. Notice the examples and the dialectical structure.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the proof from illusion
Identify why a falling drop appears as a line and a rotating flame as a circle. The author argues these cannot be explained by the external sense of sight alone.
Consider what sight perceives at a single moment versus over time.
3
Analyze the objection and reply
Follow the objector's challenge to the premise about sight, and the author's response, which includes a counter-proposal and support from Ibn Sina.
The objection uses induction as a critique; note how the reply refutes it.

Source Text

( الثاني : القطرة النازلة نراها خطاً) مستقيماً ( والشعلة التي تدار بسرعة شديدة نراها كالدائرة وليستا ) أي القطرة والشعلة ( في الخارج عن القوى المدركة ( خطاً ودائرة فهو أي كونهما كذلك إنما يكون ( في الحس المشترك وليس في الباصرة لأنها إنما تدرك الشيء حيث هو ) حتى إذا زال عن مكانه لم تدركه فيه بل في مكان آخر فقط ( فهو لارتسامهما على الوجه المذكور ( في قوة أخرى سوى الباصرة، ( وليست ) تلك القوة ( هي النفس ( الناطقة لاستحالة اتصافها بما له مقدار ( فهي قوة جسمانية ) باطنة ترتسم فيها صور المحسوسات . ( ولقائل أن يقول : يجوز أن يكون ذلك لارتسامه في القوة (الباصرة وما ذكرتموه من أن الباصرة لا تدرك الشيء إلا حيث هو ممنوع ، إذ لا دليل عليه سوى الاستقراء الذي لا يفيد اليقين. فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن ينطبع في الباصرة صورة الجسم في حيز وقبل أن تنمحى هذه الصورة عنها تنطبع فيها صورته في حيز آخر ، وإذا اجتمعت الصورتان في الباصرة شعرت بهما معاً على أنهما صورة واحدة لشيء واحد ممتد على الاستقامة أو الاستدارة، ويؤدي ذلك أن ابن سينا يسلم أن البصر يدرك الحركة ويستحيل إدراكها إلا على الوجه الذي صورناه، وأيضاً ارتسام ما له امتداد في النفس إنما يستحيل إذا كان حلول الصور فيها كحلول الأعراض في محالها وهو مما ينازع فيه لأن الأعراض متمانعة دون الصور.
(The second: the falling drop we see as a straight line, and the flame that is whirled rapidly we see as a circle, while they are not—that is, the drop and the flame—in external reality a line and a circle. So their being so is only in the common sense, not in the sight, because sight only perceives a thing where it is; when it departs from its place, it does not perceive it there but only in another place. So it is due to their imprinting in the aforementioned manner in another faculty besides sight. And that faculty is not the rational soul, because it is impossible for it to be characterized by that which has magnitude; so it is a corporeal faculty, internal, in which images of sensibles are imprinted. And someone may say: it is possible that that is due to imprinting in the sight faculty, and what you mentioned that sight only perceives a thing where it is is not granted, for there is no proof for it except induction, which does not yield certainty. So we say: why is it not possible that the image of a body in one place is imprinted in the sight, and before this image is erased from it, its image in another place is imprinted in it, and when the two images come together in the sight, it senses them together as a single image for a single thing extended in a straight or curved line? And this is supported by the fact that Ibn Sina affirms that sight perceives motion, and it is impossible to perceive motion except in the manner we have described. Also, the imprinting of something with extension in the soul is impossible only if the indwelling of images in it is like the indwelling of accidents in their loci, which is disputed because accidents are mutually exclusive while images are not.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the existence of the common sense faculty. The proof uses two perceptual phenomena: a falling drop of water appears as a continuous straight line, and a rotating flame appears as a circle. In reality, the drop is not a line and the flame is not a circle; they are only perceived as such. The author argues that this perception cannot be due to sight (bāṣira) because sight only perceives an object where it actually is at a given moment. The drop moves through different positions, yet we see a continuous line; similarly for the flame. Therefore, there must be an internal faculty (the common sense) in which multiple instantaneous images are imprinted together, creating the perception of a continuous line or circle. This internal faculty is corporeal, not the rational soul, because the soul is incorporeal and cannot have images with magnitude. The passage then addresses an objection: perhaps the sight faculty itself can retain multiple images and combine them. The objector denies that sight only perceives a thing where it is, calling it an unproven inductive generalization. The author replies by proposing that the sight could indeed retain successive images and combine them into one perceived extension. He supports this by citing Ibn Sina (Avicenna), who holds that sight perceives motion—a phenomenon that requires successive images. The author also notes that the impossibility of images with extension in the soul is only if images inhere like accidents, a position that is contested, because accidents are mutually exclusive while images are not.

الباصرة
النفس الناطقة

الاستقراء
ابن سينا

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perceptual illusions like a falling drop appearing as a line are just errors of the eye.
Correction: The author shows that these cannot be explained by sight alone and require an internal faculty where multiple images combine.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the falling drop and rotating flame cannot be perceived as a line and circle by sight alone?
A) Sight perceives only where the object is at a single moment, so it cannot combine multiple positions into a continuous shape.
B) Sight is too slow to follow moving objects.
C) The drop and flame are not real objects.
D) The common sense is located in the brain, not the eye.

Key Takeaway
The falling drop and rotating flame illusions demonstrate that we possess an internal common sense that combines successive sensory imprints into continuous shapes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a flowing river, do you ever realize that your eyes only capture still images and it is your internal sense that creates the flow? How does that internal mechanism affect the reliability of your everyday experience? What would it mean if your 'common sense' sometimes constructs shapes that do not exist in reality — how might that affect your trust in your own perceptions when it comes to matters of faith?
?
The author insists that your rational soul cannot contain images with magnitude. Yet you constantly recall the shape of your childhood home or the face of a loved one. Where, then, do these extended images reside? If they are in a corporeal faculty, what does that say about your true self and the seat of your consciousness? Are you willing to accept that part of your identity is housed in a physical faculty that can be damaged or decay?
?
This proof shows that you have an internal sense that combines discrete snapshots into a seamless reality. If your internal sense can be tricked (by a rotating flame into seeing a circle that isn't there), how can you be certain that your most basic perceptions of the world are not illusory? In what areas of your life have you trusted your senses uncritically, and what would it take to verify them?

Third Proof: Dreams, Fevers, and Visions
Presents the third proof using dreams, fevers, and visions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how dreams and visions provide evidence for an internal corporeal faculty, the common sense.

Core Idea
Dreams show that perceptions exist within us, not in the external world.

The author presents a compelling proof based on everyday experience: sleepers, feverish patients, and diviners perceive vivid images and sounds that are clearly real to them, yet are unseen by others. This forces us to posit an internal faculty within the perceiver that apprehends these forms. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the detailed description of the sleeper's experience.
2
Identify the key claim and reasoning
Find the central claim: the images seen in dreams must exist somewhere. Then trace the reasoning: they are not external (since others don't see them), so they must be internal. Why must the internal faculty be corporeal?
The proof uses the principle that pure non-existence cannot be distinguished.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that there must be a separate faculty (the common sense) that receives images when external senses are inactive. Ask yourself why this conclusion is necessary.
This connects to the overall kalam theory of perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : ما يراه النائم والمبرسم والكاهن موجود فإن كل واحد منهم يشاهد صوراً محسوسة ويدرك أصواتا مسموعة بحيث لا يرتاب فيها ويميز بينها وبين غيرها فلا بد أن يكون لتلك الصور والأصوات وجود إذ العدم المحض يستحيل أن يتميز عن غيره ويشاهد على حسب ما تشاهد الأمور الموجودة وليس) وجودها (في الخارج وإلا رآها كل سليم الحس فهو في المدرك (وهو) أي ذلك المدرك ( جسماني) لا عقلي ( لما مر ) من أن الجزئيات لا تدركها إلا قوى جسمانية وليس حساً ظاهراً لتعطله في النوم ولأن الرائي ربما كان مغموض العينين فوجب أن يكون العقل فوجب أن يكون هناك قوة أخرى ترتسم فيها تلك الصور عند كون المادة غائبة. قلنا : ذلك ممنوع أيضاً إذ لا یری الناقصة من نظر إلى الشمس نظراً في زمان ممتداً وإلى روضة مخضرة ساعة طويلة كما مر في صدر النوع الأول، فإن تلك القصة تدل على أن في الحواس الظاهرة نوعاً آخر للارتسام بحيث لا يقتضي حضور الماهية وإنه يجوز أن يرتسم في الحواس الظاهرة صور لا منه قبل المحسوس أصلاً كما ارتسم في الحس المشترك على ما زعمتم ولا بد لنفي ذلك من دليل . قوله: (وأيضا ) إشارة إلى أنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك لارتسامه في النفس المجردة. وقوله : لأن الأعراض ممانعة دون الصور فإن حصول السواد في محل ينافي حصول البياض في ذلك المحل مع أنه لا يتصور التنافي بين صورتيهما أصلاً.
The third proof: what the sleeper, the feverish, and the seer see exists, for each of them observes sensible images and perceives audible sounds in such a way that he does not doubt them and distinguishes them from others. So there must be existence for those images and sounds, since pure non-existence cannot be distinguished from others and observed in the manner that existing things are observed. Their existence is not in external reality, otherwise every sound-sensed person would see them. So it is in the perceiver, and that perceiver is corporeal, not intellectual, as previously stated, that particulars are only perceived by corporeal faculties. And it is not an external sense, because it is inoperative during sleep, and because the seer may have closed eyes. So it must be another faculty in which those images are imprinted when the matter is absent. We say: that is also not granted, for he who looks at the sun for an extended time and at a green meadow for a long time, as mentioned earlier in the beginning of the first type, shows that in the external senses there is another kind of imprinting that does not require the presence of the essence, and that it is possible for images to be imprinted in the external senses that were not from any sensible before, just as they are imprinted in the common sense according to your claim, and without a proof, that cannot be denied. His statement: 'also' indicates that it is possible that this is due to imprinting in the abstract soul. And his statement: 'because accidents are mutually exclusive but images are not'—for the occurrence of blackness in a locus contradicts the occurrence of whiteness in that same locus, while there is no contradiction between their two images at all.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the existence of the common sense, based on dreams, fevers, and prophetic visions. The argument states that people in these states perceive images and sounds that are vivid and distinguishable, so they must have some reality. However, these perceived objects are not present in the external world (since others do not perceive them). Therefore, they must exist within the perceiver himself, in some internal faculty. This faculty must be corporeal, not intellectual, because particulars are only perceived by corporeal faculties, and it cannot be an external sense because those are inactive during sleep or when eyes are closed. Thus, there must be a separate internal faculty (the common sense) that receives such images in the absence of external matter. The passage then addresses an objection: perhaps external senses can also retain images without the presence of the object, as in afterimages (e.g., after looking at the sun or a green meadow). The author replies that this is possible, but it does not disprove the existence of a separate internal faculty; rather, it shows that external senses have a kind of imprinting that doesn't require the object's presence. The objection is therefore not granted without proof. The passage ends with two glosses: one noting that an objector might attribute such images to the abstract soul, and another clarifying that accidents are mutually exclusive while images are not, so the soul could potentially hold multiple images.

المبرسم
الكاهن
العدم المحض
المدرك الجسماني

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dream images have no reality at all; they are pure nothing.
Correction: The passage argues that pure non-existence cannot be distinguished or perceived, so dream images must have some mode of existence within the perceiver's faculty.

Checkpoint

A) Because such images are only seen by the sleeper and not by others, yet they are clearly perceived.
B) Because dreams are caused by the devil.
C) Because the senses are inactive during sleep.
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
Dreams and visions prove that there is a corporeal faculty within us that perceives forms when external senses are inactive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The feverish person sees things others do not—yet they are certain of what they see. Have you ever been absolutely certain of something that others dismissed, only to later discover your certainty was based on an inner conviction? How do you distinguish true inner perception from deception? Ask Allah for clarity and sincerity.
?
The argument says pure non-existence cannot be distinguished. Think of a sin or a reality you treat as if it does not exist—like your own mortality or a hidden flaw. What would change if you truly acknowledged its existence? Will you repent today and face it with courage?

Defending the Third Proof: Afterimages and Other Objections
Addresses objections to the third proof involving afterimages and the nature of images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that afterimages might be explained by external senses and the responses that defend the necessity of the common sense.

Core Idea
Afterimages do not disprove the common sense; they show a different kind of imprinting.

Having presented the third proof, the author anticipates an objection: perhaps the images seen in dreams could be due to a form of retention in the external senses, like afterimages from staring at the sun. The author replies that this does not negate the need for a separate internal faculty, especially since such afterimages are still sensible. Additionally, two more objections are considered: that images might be in the abstract soul, and that images unlike accidents are not mutually exclusive. Let's follow the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the passage from 'We say: that is also not granted' to the end. Note how the objector uses the afterimage phenomenon to challenge the need for a common sense.
The author's reply is not a complete rejection but a nuanced response.
2
Understand the author's first point
The author argues that afterimages show a different kind of imprinting in external senses, but this does not disprove the common sense; without proof, the objection is not granted.
3
Analyze the two glosses
The passage ends with two additional points: one suggesting images could be in the soul, and another clarifying that images (unlike accidents) do not mutually exclude each other. Consider how these relate to the main argument.
4
Connect to the overall proof
Reflect on how the responses strengthen the original proof. The common sense remains necessary because external senses are inactive during sleep, and the soul can hold multiple images without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : ما يراه النائم والمبرسم والكاهن موجود فإن كل واحد منهم يشاهد صوراً محسوسة ويدرك أصواتا مسموعة بحيث لا يرتاب فيها ويميز بينها وبين غيرها فلا بد أن يكون لتلك الصور والأصوات وجود إذ العدم المحض يستحيل أن يتميز عن غيره ويشاهد على حسب ما تشاهد الأمور الموجودة وليس) وجودها (في الخارج وإلا رآها كل سليم الحس فهو في المدرك (وهو) أي ذلك المدرك ( جسماني) لا عقلي ( لما مر ) من أن الجزئيات لا تدركها إلا قوى جسمانية وليس حساً ظاهراً لتعطله في النوم ولأن الرائي ربما كان مغموض العينين فوجب أن يكون العقل فوجب أن يكون هناك قوة أخرى ترتسم فيها تلك الصور عند كون المادة غائبة. قلنا : ذلك ممنوع أيضاً إذ لا یری الناقصة من نظر إلى الشمس نظراً في زمان ممتداً وإلى روضة مخضرة ساعة طويلة كما مر في صدر النوع الأول، فإن تلك القصة تدل على أن في الحواس الظاهرة نوعاً آخر للارتسام بحيث لا يقتضي حضور الماهية وإنه يجوز أن يرتسم في الحواس الظاهرة صور لا منه قبل المحسوس أصلاً كما ارتسم في الحس المشترك على ما زعمتم ولا بد لنفي ذلك من دليل . قوله: (وأيضا ) إشارة إلى أنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك لارتسامه في النفس المجردة. وقوله : لأن الأعراض ممانعة دون الصور فإن حصول السواد في محل ينافي حصول البياض في ذلك المحل مع أنه لا يتصور التنافي بين صورتيهما أصلاً.
The third proof: what the sleeper, the feverish, and the seer see exists, for each of them observes sensible images and perceives audible sounds in such a way that he does not doubt them and distinguishes them from others. So there must be existence for those images and sounds, since pure non-existence cannot be distinguished from others and observed in the manner that existing things are observed. Their existence is not in external reality, otherwise every sound-sensed person would see them. So it is in the perceiver, and that perceiver is corporeal, not intellectual, as previously stated, that particulars are only perceived by corporeal faculties. And it is not an external sense, because it is inoperative during sleep, and because the seer may have closed eyes. So it must be another faculty in which those images are imprinted when the matter is absent. We say: that is also not granted, for he who looks at the sun for an extended time and at a green meadow for a long time, as mentioned earlier in the beginning of the first type, shows that in the external senses there is another kind of imprinting that does not require the presence of the essence, and that it is possible for images to be imprinted in the external senses that were not from any sensible before, just as they are imprinted in the common sense according to your claim, and without a proof, that cannot be denied. His statement: 'also' indicates that it is possible that this is due to imprinting in the abstract soul. And his statement: 'because accidents are mutually exclusive but images are not'—for the occurrence of blackness in a locus contradicts the occurrence of whiteness in that same locus, while there is no contradiction between their two images at all.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the existence of the common sense, based on dreams, fevers, and prophetic visions. The argument states that people in these states perceive images and sounds that are vivid and distinguishable, so they must have some reality. However, these perceived objects are not present in the external world (since others do not perceive them). Therefore, they must exist within the perceiver himself, in some internal faculty. This faculty must be corporeal, not intellectual, because particulars are only perceived by corporeal faculties, and it cannot be an external sense because those are inactive during sleep or when eyes are closed. Thus, there must be a separate internal faculty (the common sense) that receives such images in the absence of external matter. The passage then addresses an objection: perhaps external senses can also retain images without the presence of the object, as in afterimages (e.g., after looking at the sun or a green meadow). The author replies that this is possible, but it does not disprove the existence of a separate internal faculty; rather, it shows that external senses have a kind of imprinting that doesn't require the object's presence. The objection is therefore not granted without proof. The passage ends with two glosses: one noting that an objector might attribute such images to the abstract soul, and another clarifying that accidents are mutually exclusive while images are not, so the soul could potentially hold multiple images.

Key Terms
النفس المجردة
الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Afterimages prove that external senses alone can account for all internal perceptions, so there is no need for a separate common sense.
Correction: The author replies that afterimages show a different kind of imprinting, but they do not explain perceptions during sleep when external senses are inactive; thus the common sense remains necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the afterimage example not disprove the common sense?
A) Afterimages are not real perceptions.
B) Afterimages are imprinted differently and the common sense is still needed for dreams.
C) Afterimages are caused by the common sense.
D) The objector's argument is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The objection from afterimages does not undermine the third proof; it merely shows a different mode of imprinting, while the common sense remains necessary for internal perceptions during sleep.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you wake from a vivid dream, do you ever consider it mere remnants of your day, or could it be a genuine perception from an inner faculty? This lesson insists there is a common sense separate from external senses. How often do you dismiss your inner experiences as 'just imagination' rather than taking them seriously?
?
The author argues that images, unlike physical things, can coexist without conflict. Think of a time you held two contradictory emotions or thoughts simultaneously. Does that experience confirm the non-physical nature of your soul? How does this affect the way you understand your own internal conflicts?
?
You trust your eyes every day, but afterimages show they can produce images without an object present. When have you mistaken a mental image for reality? Are you prepared to examine your own perceptions with the same critical eye this lesson applies to afterimages?
