The Estimative Power: Definition and Objection
Al-Iji defines the estimative power with examples and raises an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the estimative power (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya), its function in perceiving particular meanings, and an important objection about its capacity for complex judgments.

Core Idea
The estimative power perceives non-sensory meanings like enmity and affection, not sensory qualities.

This passage introduces one of the internal faculties of perception: the estimative power. Al-Iji defines it clearly with vivid examples from animal behavior, then sharpens our understanding by raising a subtle objection about how it can make judgments involving relations. Pay close attention to how the objection challenges the scope of this faculty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the full meaning.
Notice the examples of the sheep and the lamb.
2
Identify the definition and examples
Find where the passage defines the estimative power and note the two examples given. Understand what 'particular meanings' means.
These are non-sensory but attached to sensory objects.
3
Analyze the objection
Focus on the objection: why can't the estimative power (or common sense) alone produce the judgment 'this smaller is this sweeter'? Identify the three components needed for judgment.
The objection points to a problem of perceiving both terms and the relation.
4
Reflect on the outcome
Consider what the objection implies about the estimative power's limitations. Does it mean the power is insufficient? Or does it point to another faculty?
The passage does not give the answer; it leaves the objection open.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : ( من تلك القوى هي (القوة الوهمية وهي التي تدرك المعاني الجزئية ) المتعلقة بالصور المحسوسة ( كالعداوة ) الجزئية ( التي تدركها الشاة من الذئب ) فتهرب منه ( والمحبة ) الجزئية ( التي تدركها السخلة من أمها ) فتميل إليها . فإن هذه المعاني لا بد لها من قوة مدركة سوى الناطقة . قالوا : ( وهي التي تحكم بأن هذا الأصغر) هو ( هذا الحلو ) ويتجه عليه أن النسبة التي بينهما وإن كانت معنى جزئياً مدركاً للقوة الوهمية إلا أن طرفيها محسوسان ومدركان بالحس المشترك والحكم لا بد أن يدرك الطرفين والنسبة حتى يتمكن من الحكم عليها فلا يجوز أن يكون الحكم المذكور للقوة الوهمية ولا للحس المشترك .
The third (of those powers is the estimative power) which perceives particular meanings related to sensible forms, (like the particular enmity) that the sheep perceives from the wolf, so it flees from it, (and the particular affection) that the lamb perceives from its mother, so it inclines toward them. For these meanings must have a perceiving power other than the rational [soul]. They said: (And it is that which judges that this smaller) is (this sweeter). And it is objected against this that the relation between them, although it is a particular meaning perceived by the estimative power, yet its two terms are sensible and perceived by the common sense, and judgment must perceive the two terms and the relation in order to be able to judge upon them; thus it is not permissible for the mentioned judgment to belong to the estimative power nor to the common sense.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage defines the third internal power, al-quwwa al-wahmiyya (the estimative power). It begins by stating that it perceives particular meanings (ma'ani juz'iyya) that are attached to sensible forms. Two examples are given: the sheep perceives enmity from the wolf and flees; the lamb perceives affection from its mother and inclines toward her. These meanings are not sensible themselves but are particular. The author notes that these meanings require a perceiving power distinct from the rational soul (al-natiqa). Then an objection is raised: the estimative power is said to judge that 'this smaller is this sweeter'. The objection points out that the relation between the two (the smallness and sweetness) is a particular meaning, but its two terms (the small thing and the sweet thing) are sensible and perceived by the common sense (hiss mushtarak). For a judgment to occur, the judging faculty must perceive both the terms and the relation. Therefore, the judgment cannot be attributed solely to the estimative power or to the common sense. This objection highlights a potential limitation of the estimative power's role in complex judgments.

Key Terms
القوة الوهمية
المعاني الجزئية
الحس المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the estimative power perceives sensory qualities like color or shape.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it perceives particular non-sensory meanings (e.g., enmity, affection) that are attached to sensible objects.

Checkpoint
What is the function of the estimative power (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya) according to this passage?
A) It perceives sensible forms.
B) It perceives particular meanings related to sensible forms, like enmity and affection.
C) It judges between two sensible objects.
D) It preserves memories of past experiences.

Key Takeaway
The estimative power perceives particular non-sensory meanings, but its role in complex judgments like 'this smaller is this sweeter' is problematic because it does not perceive the sensible terms of the relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sense that someone dislikes you, do you ever stop to ask whether that perception is from your estimative power or from the rational soul? How often do you act on such feelings without critically examining them, and what has that cost you?
?
The passage shows that a simple judgment like 'this smaller is this sweeter' raises a deep problem about how we integrate different kinds of knowledge. In your own life, when you make quick judgments about people or situations, are you aware of the different sources of information you're combining? What would it mean for you to bring more rational soul into your everyday judgments?
?
Consider the sheep's perception of the wolf's enmity. That perception leads to flight and preservation. But in your human life, you have the rational soul. How often do your immediate perceptions of threat or affection dictate your actions, bypassing your intellect? What is one relationship where you need to pause and let your rational soul assess before acting?

The Retentive and Imaginative Powers
Al-Iji defines the retentive and imaginative powers and answers an objection about their interaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct functions of the retentive and imaginative powers, how the imaginative power is used by both the intellect and estimative power, and the analogical mirroring among internal senses.

Core Idea
The imaginative power is a versatile tool used by both intellect and estimation.

Al-Iji continues his enumeration of the internal senses, defining two more powers: the retentive (hafiza) and the imaginative (mutakhayyila). He explains their roles relative to the estimative power and common sense, and then addresses a subtle objection about how the estimative power can direct the imaginative power in dealing with sensory forms. This passage reveals the layered structure of the inner faculties and their interactions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the two new powers defined: the retentive (hafiza) and the imaginative (mutakhayyila).
The passage is a continuous explanation; let the author's order guide you.
2
Understand the retentive power's analogy
The retentive power preserves meanings perceived by the estimative power, like a treasury. Its relation to the estimative power is compared to the imagination (khayal) to common sense.
This analogy helps situate the hafiza within the internal sense hierarchy.
3
Examine the imaginative power's operation
The imaginative power disposes over sensible forms and particular meanings by combining and separating them, as in the examples given. This unique power is distinct from other senses.
Note that it works on both forms and meanings, not just images.
4
Grasp the naming shift: mutakhayyila vs. mufakkira
When the intellect uses this power, it is called thinking (mufakkira); when the estimative power uses it in sensibles, it is called imaginative (mutakhayyila). This shows its dual service.
5
Analyze the objection and answer
The objection asks how the estimative power can use the imaginative power in sensibles if it does not perceive them. The answer uses the mirror analogy for internal powers, with the estimative power as sultan.
This explains the interaction and hierarchy of internal faculties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة : ( منها ( القوة الحافظة وهي الحافظة للمعاني التي تدركها القوة ( الوهمية كالخزانة لها ونسبتها إلى الوهمية نسبة الخيال إلى الحس المشترك، فاستغنى في إثباتها (بما ذكرناه، ثم ) الخامسة : القوة ( المتخيلة وهي ( القوة ( التي تتصرف في الصور المحسوسة والمعاني ) الجزئية المنتزعة منها وتصرفها فيها (بالتركيب ) تارة ( والتفصيل) أخرى (مثل إنسان ذي رأسين وإنسان عديم الرأس وحيوان نصفه إنسان ونصفه فرس) وهذا التصرف غير ثابت لسائر الحواس والقوى فهو لقوة أخرى، وهذه القوة إذا استعملها العقل) في مدركاته بضم بعضها إلى بعض أو فصله عنه ( سميت مفكرة ) كما أنها إذا استعملها الوهم في المحسوسات مطلقاً سميت متخيلة. فإن قيل: كيف يستعملها الوهم في الصور المحسوسة مع أنه ليس مدركاً لها؟ أجيب : بأن القوى الباطنة كالمرايا المتقابلة فينعكس إلى كل منها ما ارتسم في الأخرى والوهمية هي سلطان تلك القوى فلها تصرف في مدركاتها واستعمال ما هو آلة فيها بل لها تسلط على مدركات العاقلة فتنازعها فيها وتحكم عليها بخلاف أحكامها فمن سخرها للقوة العقلية بحيث صارت مطاوعة لها فقد فاز فوزاً عظيماً
The fourth (of them is the retentive power) and it is the preserver of the meanings that the estimative power perceives, like a treasury for it; its relationship to the estimative power is like the relationship of the imagination (al-khayal) to the common sense. Thus they dispensed with proving it (by what we have mentioned). Then the fifth: the imaginative power, and it is the power that disposes in the sensible forms and the particular meanings extracted from them, disposing among them (by combination) sometimes (and by separation) other times, (like a human with two heads, a human without a head, an animal half human and half horse). This disposition is not established for the other senses and powers, so it belongs to another power. And this power, when the intellect uses it in its perceptions by joining some of them to others or separating from it, it is called the thinking power (mufakkira), just as when the estimative power uses it in sensibles absolutely, it is called the imaginative power (mutakhayyila). If it is said: How does the estimative power use it in sensible forms when it is not a perceiver of them? The answer: The internal powers are like facing mirrors, so what is impressed in one is reflected to each of them, and the estimative power is the sultan of those powers; thus it has disposal over its perceptions and the use of what is an instrument in them. Rather, it has domination over the perceptions of the rational soul, so it disputes with it about them and judges them contrary to its judgments. Whoever subjugates it to the rational power so that it becomes obedient to it has achieved a great success.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage continues the enumeration of internal powers. It first describes the fourth power, al-quwwa al-hafiza (the retentive power), which preserves the meanings perceived by the estimative power, acting as its treasury. Its relation to the estimative power is analogous to the relation of the imaginal faculty (al-khayal) to the common sense. The author says they are satisfied with the proof already given. Then the fifth power is al-mutakhayyila (the imaginative power), which disposes (tasarruf) in sensible forms and particular meanings by combining and separating them, like creating images of a two-headed human or a half-human half-horse. This disposition is not found in other powers, so it must be a distinct faculty. When the intellect uses this power in its perceptions, it is called mufakkira (the thinking power); when the estimative power uses it in sensibles, it is called mutakhayyila. An objection arises: how can the estimative power use the imaginative power in sensible forms if the estimative power itself does not perceive sensibles? The answer explains that the internal powers are like facing mirrors; what is in one is reflected in another. The estimative power is the sultan (ruler) over these powers, so it can dispose in their perceptions and use them as instruments. It even dominates the rational perceptions and sometimes judges contrary to reason. The one who subdues this imaginative power to the rational soul has achieved great success.

Key Terms
القوة الحافظة
القوة المتخيلة
القوة المفكرة
التصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imaginative power is only concerned with sensory images and is fully under rational control.
Correction: It also deals with particular meanings extracted from senses, and it can be used by the estimative power or intellect; if not subdued to reason, it may oppose rational judgments.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the imaginative power (al-mutakhayyila) come to be called 'thinking power' (mufakkira)?
A) When it is used by the estimative power in sensibles.
B) When it is used by the intellect in its perceptions.
C) When it combines images without any external direction.
D) When it separates meanings from forms.

Key Takeaway
The internal powers interact like mirrors, with the estimative power as their ruler, and the imaginative power serves both intellectual and estimative uses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your imagination presents you with a fear or desire that contradicts what you know to be true, do you let it rule you or do you subjugate it to your intellect? What would it take for you to achieve the 'great success' mentioned in this lesson?
?
Are you aware of the internal battle within yourself—the estimative power trying to dominate your rational soul? In what specific area of your life have you let your estimations override your intellect, and what will you do to change that starting today?
?
The estimative power can judge contrary to reason. Is there a belief you hold about yourself, others, or Allah that you suspect comes from your estimative power rather than your intellect? How can you verify which power is speaking?

The Proof and Refutation of Internal Powers from Multiple Acts
Al-Iji presents a proof and its refutation regarding internal powers and multiplicity of acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for internal powers based on the multiplicity of acts and the critical response that a single power can produce multiple acts via different instruments.

Core Idea
Multiple acts do not necessarily prove multiple powers; one power can act through many instruments.

This passage from al-Iji's Mawāqif introduces a key argument for the existence of five internal powers: the multiplicity of distinct acts requires multiple powers, based on the principle that a simple cause produces a single effect. However, al-Iji immediately raises a powerful objection: why cannot one power produce multiple acts through different instruments or conditions? This lesson explores both sides of this debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its full English translation. Pay attention to the list of five acts mentioned: perception of sensibles, perception of particular meanings, preservation, and disposition.
Notice that the passage includes a lexical clarification before the refutation.
2
Identify the principle used in the proof
The proof relies on the principle 'from the one only the one proceeds' (الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد). Highlight how this connects the multiplicity of acts to the multiplicity of powers.
3
Examine the lexical clarifications
Al-Iji clarifies three terms: 'muzarrad', 'li-tasaddufihi', and 'la harisa'. Understand their meanings, but note they are not central to the main argument.
These clarifications might be obscure; focus on the overall logic.
4
Understand the refutation
The refutation argues that even if the principle is accepted, a single power with multiple instruments or conditions can produce multiple acts. This weakens the proof.
This refutation is often used elsewhere in kalam, showing inconsistency if not accepted here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنختم هذا النوع : ) الثاني ( بأبحاث : الأول : عرف وجود هذه القوى الخمس الباطنية ( بتعدد الأفعال ) الخمسة التي هي إدراك المحسوسات وإدراك المعاني الجزئية المتعلقة بها وحفظهما والتصرف فيهما ( لما اعتقدوا أنه لا يصدر عن الواحد إلا الواحد، وقد عرفت مافيه ) قوله : (مزرد) بالزاي المعجمة والراء المهملة وهو هاهنا من باب التفعيل يقال : زرده أي خنقه وهذا بالخاء المعجمة وبالنون والقاف . وقوله : لتصادفه بالفاء من المصادفة. وقوله : إذ لا حارس هناك أي لا حافظ هناك . ( ثم ) إن سلمنا صحته قلنا : ( لم لا يجوز أن تكون القوة واحدة والآلات متعددة أو الشرائط ( فتصدر تلك الأفعال عنها بحسب تعددها كما جوزتموه في مواضيع أخرى
And let us conclude this (second) type with investigations: The first: They knew the existence of these five internal powers (by the multiplicity of the five acts), which are: perception of sensibles, perception of particular meanings related to them, preserving both of them, and disposing in both of them, because they believed that from the one only the one proceeds. And you have known what is in it. His saying: (muzarrad) with the dotted za' and the unimportant ra', and it is here from the form of taf'il; it is said: zaradahu meaning he choked him, and this is with dotted kha', nun, and qaf. And his saying: li-tasaddufihi with fa' from al-musadafa. And his saying: idh la harisa hunak meaning la hafiza hunak. (Then) if we grant its correctness, we say: (Why is it not permissible that the power be one and the instruments be many or the conditions, so that those acts proceed from it according to their multiplicity, just as you have allowed it in other topics?
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage introduces the first investigation (al-bahth al-awwal) of the second type (internal powers). It begins by stating that the existence of the five internal powers is known through the multiplicity of five acts: perception of sensibles, perception of particular meanings, preservation of both, and disposition in both. The argument is based on the principle that from one thing only one thing proceeds (the principle that a simple cause produces a single effect). Since the acts are multiple, there must be multiple powers. The author adds a note: 'and you have known what is in it' implying this principle is not universally accepted. Then a lexical clarification follows: the word 'muzarrad' (with za and ra) means 'choked' and is from the form taf'il; also explains 'li-tasaddufihi' and 'la harisa'. Then the author presents a critique: even if we accept the principle's validity, why can't one power produce multiple acts through different instruments or conditions? This is allowed in other topics. Thus the proof is weak.

Key Terms
تعدد الأفعال
الواحد
الآلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The multiplicity of acts necessarily proves the multiplicity of powers.
Correction: The passage shows that a single power can produce multiple acts through different instruments or conditions, so the proof is not absolute.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the proof for the multiplicity of internal powers according to this passage?
A) Direct observation of brain damage.
B) The principle that from one only one proceeds, coupled with the multiplicity of acts.
C) The authority of earlier scholars.
D) The need for separate powers for each sense.

Key Takeaway
The proof from multiple acts for multiple internal powers is undermined by the possibility that a single power can act through multiple instruments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a person's multiple good deeds must come from multiple separate motives? How might a single sincere intention, expressed through different actions, challenge that assumption? Are you willing to re-examine your own heart for such hidden assumptions?
?
The principle 'from the one only one proceeds' is contested. What principles do you hold as absolutely certain in your life—perhaps about God, about yourself, or about others—that might actually need re-examination? What would it cost you to question them?
?
If a single power can act through many instruments, then the diversity you see might stem from a unified source. How does this change the way you look at the differences among people or the events in your life? Can you trace the multiple streams of your own experience back to a single Origin? What is that Origin for you?
